** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GREGORY YOO BAIK NAGAO
File: AXXX XX0 390 - Honolulu, HI
September 21, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Emmanuel B. Guerrero, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Heith M. Kaneshige
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony pursuant to section 101(a)(43)(F), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(F)º
 
Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony pursuant to section 101(a)(43)(G), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(G)º
 
Sec. 237(a)(2)(C), I&N ACt ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(C)º - Convicted of firearms or destructive device violation
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
This case was last before us on July 24, 2006, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from an Immigration Judge’s decision finding that he did not derive citizenship through his mother or through his United States citizen stepfather, who adopted him in 1950. Our decision left in place a previous Immigration Judge decision finding the respondent removable as an alien convicted of an aggravated felony and ordering his removal from the United States. The respondent’s petition to the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, seeking review of our July 2006 decision, was dismissed.
 
The respondent is a native and citizen of South Korea. He has timely appealed the Immigration Judge’s July 17, 2009, decision rejecting his motion to reopen because the Immigration Court lacked jurisdiction to consider this motion, as the Board last dismissed his appeal in July 2006. The respondent also has filed a motion to reopen with this Board, arguing that proceedings should be reopened to allow him to pursue adjustment of status though the approved visa petition filed on his behalf by his United States citizen wife. The appeal is dismissed and the motion to reopen is denied.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s factual determinations, including credibility determinations, for clear error. See United States v. National Assn. of Real Estate Boards, 339 U.S. 485, 495 (1950) (a factual finding is not “clearly erroneous” merely because there are two permissible views of the evidence). The Board reviews de novo questions of law, discretion, judgment, and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges. Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
We first note that we find no cause to disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision that she had no jurisdiction to consider the respondent’s May 11, 2009, motion to reopen. Because the respondent had appealed the Immigration Judge’s last decision of record, jurisdiction over any subsequent motions would rest with the Board, and not the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2, 1003.23(b)(1).
 
*2 Turning to the motion to remand, the respondent claims that reopening is appropriate to assess his eligibility for “newly-acquired relief” in the form of an application for adjustment of status based on an approved visa petition filed by his United States citizen wife. See Respondent’s Brief and Motion at 6. Because the respondent’s motion was filed with the Board on September 8, 2009, it is untimely, as it was filed some 3 years after our last decision in this case, which was issued in July 2006. Generally, a motion to reopen must be filed no later than 90 days after the final administrative decision was rendered. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2).
 
The respondent argues that the time period for filing the motion should be tolled because his prior counsel provided ineffective assistance in (1) pursuing the argument that removal proceedings should be terminated because the respondent had derived United States citizenship through his parents, (2) failing to pursue below a challenge to the validity of the respondent’s convictions, and (3) failing to pursue below a waiver of inadmissibility under former section 212(c) of the Act. See Respondent’s Brief and Motion at 7-8.
 
While the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, under whose jurisdiction this case arises, recognizes equitable tolling of motions deadlines in ineffective assistance of counsel cases, an alien must demonstrate not only that his constitutional right to due process has been violated by the conduct of counsel, but that the alien has exercised due diligence in pursuing the case during the period the alien seeks to toll. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 897 (9th Cir. 2003) (holding that equitable tolling will be applied where the alien is prevented from timely filing a motion by deception, fraud, or error so long as the alien acted with due diligence in discovering the deception, fraud, or error). Moreover, an alien must demonstrate substantial compliance with the general procedural requirements for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) (requiring the alien to lay out, in a detailed affidavit, the agreement between the alien and the representative, to give the representative the opportunity to respond to the allegation of ineffectiveness, and to file a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authority or adequately explain why no filing was made).
 
The respondent has not shown due diligence or any attempt to comply with the Matter of Lozada requirements. In fact, the respondent entirely omits any explanation for the 3-year delay in filing a motion to reopen and claims to be exempt from meeting the Lozada requirements because the ineffective assistance of former counsel was “clear on the record.” See Respondent’s Brief and Motion at 8. As to the latter claim, we find former counsel’s decision to pursue a derivative citizenship claim—as opposed to a direct or an indirect attack on the criminal convictions and resulting grounds of removability—to be distinct from what would constitute “ineffective assistance of counsel.” Indeed, counsel’s strategic decision in this regard is not the type of conduct typically held to be “ineffective assistance.” See Matter of Lei, 22 I&N Dec. 113, 127 n. 7 (BIA 1998) (citing Thorsteinsson v. INS, 724 F.2d 1365 (9th Cir. 1984)) (ineffective assistance counsel claim rejected where attorney made informed, strategic decision). In any event, such a decision would not constitute “clear” ineffective assistance obviating the need to make some attempt to comply with the Matter of Lozada, supra, requirements.
 
*3 As to due diligence, as noted above, the respondent’s motion is silent on this point. He offers no event that triggered his discovery of the alleged ineffective assistance of past counsel and does not describe his actions over the course of the 3 years that lapsed between the Board’s July 2006 decision and his first attempt to reopen proceedings before the Immigration Judge in May 2009. We note for the record that the respondent indicated that the visa petition filed by his wife was approved in August 2007. Yet since that time, the record does not reflect that the respondent did anything to pursue reopening of proceedings. In the absence of any evidence that he diligently tried to discover ineffective assistance of counsel or that he actually received any ineffective attorney advice, we decline to find that the deadline for timely filing a motion to reopen should be equitably tolled. Iturribarria v. INS, supra.
 
Finally, we note that the respondent’s motion lacks a showing of prejudice, even if we were to find that ineffective assistance of counsel occurred and that tolling was warranted because of the respondent’s diligence. That is, the respondent suffered three serious convictions, i.e., first-degree robbery, possession of a prohibited firearm, and theft. See Immigration Judge’s April 13, 2006, Order. The respondent has not shown that he is admissible to the United States, in light of these convictions, which raise the possibility that he has suffered one or more crimes involving moral turpitude. If so, his ability to secure a waiver of inadmissibility as to those convictions is doubtful, since (1) the robbery and weapons convictions were pursuant to a jury verdict, rendering him ineligible for relief under former section 212(c) of the Act, and (2) he has been found removable as an alien convicted of an aggravated felony, rendering him ineligible for relief under section 212(h) of the Act.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Patricia A. Cole
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 4035442 (BIA)

2010 WL 4035450 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: KARLA PATRICIA MEJIA-CASTILLO
File: AXXX XX0 998 - San Antonio, TX
September 20, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
John E. Ricci, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Cori White
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a citizen of Nicaragua, was ordered removed from the United States in absentia on June 28, 2005, after failing to appear at a heating. The respondent filed a motion to reopen on May 28, 2008, arguing that a non-attorney legal representative advised her not to appear at her hearing, and the proceedings should be reopened based on “exceptional circumstances.” The Immigration Judge denied the motion as untimely on July 8, 2008. On July 30, 2009, the Board remanded the record to the Immigration Judge to consider whether it was appropriate to equitably toll the 180-day period to seek reopening.¿FN1º The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion on December 29, 2009, and this appeal followed. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
Under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3), the Board defers to the factual findings of an Immigration Judge, unless they are clearly erroneous, but it retains independent judgment and discretion, subject to applicable governing standards, regarding pure questions of law and the application of a particular standard of law to those facts. Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
The Immigration Judge found that he had no authority to equitably toll the 180-day period to file a motion to reopen based on “exceptional circumstances.” In the alternative, the Immigration Judge found that the respondent did not file her motion with due diligence even assuming that equitable tolling was available. In addition, the Immigration Judge declined to reopen these proceedings sua sponte.
 
The respondent argues on appeal that the Immigration Judge did not meaningfully address the equitable tolling issue and whether she filed her motion with due diligence. We disagree. The Immigration Judge addressed the issue and stated his reasons for finding that due diligence was not exercised by the respondent. We find that the Immigration Judge afforded the respondent due process while adjudicating her motion.
 
The respondent further argues on appeal that she did not know that she was a victim of ineffective assistance counsel until she retained current counsel. Presumably, the respondent requests tolling of the 180-day period to file her motion based on “exceptional circumstances” until she retained her current representative.
 
The record contains the respondent’s affidavit. Therein, she indicates that she received notice of her hearing and was told by her former representative not to appear. She further states that her former representative told her that she could later reopen her case on account of the respondent’s pregnancy. However, the respondent does not indicate in her affidavit that her former attorney told her she had an unlimited period to file her motion to reopen.
 
*2 The Board would only find it appropriate to equitably toll the 180-day period for a reasonable amount of time. A reasonable period may have been a few months after the birth of her child. We agree with the Immigration Judge that equitably tolling that period for nearly three years is not appropriate. We find on de novo review, as did the Immigration Judge, that the respondent did not exercise due diligence in maintaining her immigration status in the United States. It follows that the Immigration Judge correctly denied the respondent’s motion as untimely. See section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C). The Immigration Judge’s decision is affirmed.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The Immigration Judge found that the respondent had complied with the procedural requirements for alleging ineffective assistance of counsel. The Immigration Judge did not address whether it was appropriate to equitably toll the 180-day period to file a motion to reopen based on “exceptional circumstances.”

IN RE: LI LI
File: AXXX XX6 254 - Miami, FL
September 17, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Tahir Mella, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Shana Belyeu
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 On April 29, 2004, the Board affirmed an Immigration Judge’s decision, in which the judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen removal proceedings, which were conducted in absentia. The Board declined to reconsider this decision on July 29, 2004, and denied a motion to reopen on November 8, 2004. Over five years later the respondent, who is from China, filed a late and number-barred motion to reopen, on February 4, 2010. Section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The motion, which is opposed by the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS”), will be denied.
 
As in previous filings, the respondent blames former attorney Joseph Muto, who has been disbarred, for her failure to appear at her removal hearing.
 
The Eleventh Circuit Court of Appeals has stated that the motion to reopen period is “mandatory and jurisdictional”, and not subject to equitable tolling, even where a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel is raised. Abdi v. U.S. Atty. Gen., 430 F.3d 1148, 1149-50 (11th Cir. 2005); see also Anin v. Reno, 188 F.3d 1273, 1278 (11th Cir. 1999)(concerning motion to reopen in absentia order).
 
The Board declines to reopen proceedings under its discretionary sua sponte authority at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). The respondent has already raised a claim against Muto; such claim was last rejected by the Board on November 8, 2004. No good explanation is provided as to why the pending motion, which makes similar assertions, was not filed for more than five years. The pending motion to reopen is, therefore, denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 4035429 (BIA)

IN RE: DWIGHT ANTHONY HUEY A.K.A. JAMES HUEY

September 17, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Thomas M. Bernstein
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i)º - Convicted of controlled substance violation
  
APPLICATION: Reopening; reconsideration
 
On March 30, 2010, an Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed. On April 15, 2010, the respondent filed a motion to reconsider and a motion to reopen, which the Department of Homeland Security opposed. The Immigration Judge denied the motions on April 27, 2010. The respondent, a lawful permanent resident who is a native and citizen of Jamaica, now appeals. The respondent’s request for a waiver of the appellate filing fee is granted. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.8(a)(3) (2010). The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent makes the same argument on appeal as in his motions to reconsider and reopen. He argues that his case should be reconsidered or reopened because (a) his counsel was ineffective; and (b) the Immigration Judge should not have considered the respondent’s New York controlled substance conviction for removal purposes because the conviction was sealed (Form EOIR-26, attached statement; Exh. 2). Based on our de novo review of the issues raised by the appeal, we see no reason to disturb the Immigration Judge’s ruling. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent did not demonstrate that he had new material evidence that was not available and could not have been discovered at his prior hearing. See section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(3). His assertions of ineffective assistance of counsel, and of counsel’s conflict of interest and trickery,¿FN1º are unavailing. The record does not indicate that he was represented by counsel before the Immigration Judge (I.J., dated April 27, 2010, at 2; Form EOIR-26, attached statement; Tr. at 17, 25). Additionally, the respondent did not present evidence that his controlled substance conviction was not valid for immigration purposes (I.J. at 2). Hence, the Immigration Judge correctly determined that the respondent did not meet the standards for reopening (I.J., dated April 27, 2010, at 1-2). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
Similarly, the respondent did not establish that reconsideration was warranted because he did not specify any errors of law or fact in the Immigration Judge’s March 30, 2010, removal order (I.J. at 2). See section 240(c)(6) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(6); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(2). As such, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s denial of the motion to reconsider. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent suggests that counsel tricked him into abandoning his cancellation of removal application because he knew the respondent was mentally incompetent (Form EOIR-26, attached statement).

2010 WL 3780645 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JUAN CARLOS CANIL-TEVELAN
File: AXXX XX3 422 - Harlingen, TX
September 15, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
E.J. Montanez, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Nicole Gonzalez
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a citizen of Guatemala, was ordered removed from the United States in absentia on April 24, 2003, after failing to appear at a hearing. He filed a motion to reopen on March 18, 2010, and the Immigration Judge denied it on April 13, 2010. The respondent filed a second motion to reopen on May 6, 2010. The Immigration Judge denied it on July 9, 2010, and this appeal followed. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
Under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3), the Board defers to the factual findings of an Immigration Judge, unless they are clearly erroneous, but it retains independent judgment and discretion, subject to applicable governing standards, regarding pure questions of law and the application of a particular standard of law to those facts. Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
The respondent argues in his Notice of Appeal that “exceptional circumstances” prevented his appearance at his hearing. He states that he hired an attorney to change venue in these proceedings from Harlingen, TX, to Los Angeles, CA. The respondent indicates that he never heard from his attorney, and he did not know that he had been ordered removed from the United States in absentia until March 2010. He argues that he is a victim of ineffective assistance of counsel, and these proceedings should be reopened.
 
The Immigration Judge found that the respondent had filed a number-barred motion to reopen. We agree. The regulation at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii) provides that an alien may file only one motion to seek reopening of an order of removal entered in absentia. Consequently, the respondent’s second motion to reopen is numerically-barred from consideration. It was also correctly found by the Immigration Judge to be an untimely request based on “exceptional circumstances.” See section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C). We further agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not satisfied the procedural requirements for a claim for reopening based on ineffective assistance of counsel notwithstanding the jurisdictional bar. See Goonsuwan v. Ashcroft, 252 F.3d 383 (5th Cir. 2001); Lara v. Trominski, 216 F.3d 487 (5th Cir. 2000); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision denying reopening of these proceedings.
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 3780645 (BIA)
End of Document

IN RE: ABD EL AZIZ MINSSY A.K.A. ABD EL AZIZ FAHMY MINSSY ZEINAB OMER MINSSY EL NASH KHOLOUD ABD EL AZIZ MINSSY OMER ABD EL AZIZ MINSSY
Files: AXXX XX5 473 - Los Angeles, CA
AXXX XX5 474
AXXX XX5 475
AXXX XX5 476
September 13, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Valerie Curtis-Diop, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Chanlee S. Sutoyo
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondents’ motion to reopen is untimely and number-barred. Section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The final administrative order in these proceedings was entered by the Immigration Judge on March 12, 2003. The respondents thereafter filed their first motion to reopen raising a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, which was denied by the Immigration Judge. They appealed that denial to the Board, which dismissed their appeal in October 2004. The respondents then filed their second motion to reopen raising an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, and that motion was denied in October 2005. Their motion to reconsider that decision was also denied, in November 2006. Over 3 1/2 years later, the respondents have filed this third motion to reopen proceedings, alleging for the third time that they are victims of ineffective assistance of counsel. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes the motion.
 
The respondents urge that the time and number limits for motions to reopen should be equitably tolled because they have been the victims of ineffective assistance of counsel both at the initial hearing and with regard to all of their subsequent motions. The motion time and number limits may be equitably tolled where an alien raises a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 897 (9th Cir. 2003). An alien must, however, demonstrate that he has been diligent in raising the claim and must show that he has been prejudiced by counsel’s conduct. Id.; Ortiz v. INS, 179 F.3d 1148, 1153 (9th Cir. 1999) (finding that prejudice must be shown for a due process challenge and that prejudice is found when the performance of counsel was so inadequate that it may have affected outcome); see also Maravilla Maravilla v. Ashcroft, 381 F.3d 855, 858 (9th Cir. 2004) (an alien can show prejudice where counsel’s performance “may have affected the outcome of the proceedings”).
 
The respondents have failed to establish that they were prejudiced in this case. They urge that they have been prejudiced because their initial counsel erroneously conceded their removability and accepted pre-conclusion voluntary departure on their behalf.¿FN1º The respondents, however, have presented no evidence that the concession of removability was unreasonable. Although they identify some arguments that possibly could have been raised to challenge the reliability of the evidence that had been received by the Immigration Court regarding the diversity visas that were alleged to have been fraudulently obtained by the respondents, and to have been subsequently revoked by the United States State Department, they have failed to demonstrate that counsel acted unreasonably in conceding removability in the face of the evidence presented by the DHS (Exhs. 2-4). Nor have they shown any likelihood that they would have prevailed in their challenges to the evidence of record, or that the outcome would have been affected if the Immigration Judge rendered a decision based on the evidence of record rather than counsel’s concession. In sum, the respondents have failed to demonstrate that they were prejudiced by their initial attorney’s actions. Consequently, we do not find that equitable tolling of the time or number limits is warranted in this case. Accordingly, the motion to reopen will be denied.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

All of the allegations of deficiencies in the ensuing motions raising the claim of ineffective assistance of counsel ultimately turn on whether the respondents have shown they were prejudiced by the actions of their initial counsel. If not, none of their subsequent claims prevail.

2010 WL 3780656 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CELESTINO PELAYO RENTERIA
File: AXXX XX4 329 - Orlando, FL
September 13, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Michaelangelo Rosario, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision dated July 15, 2009. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen proceedings in which he was ordered removed in absentia on November 6, 2008. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact for clear error, but questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues in appeals, are reviewed de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii).
 
We find that proper notice was provided to the respondent. The record reflects that the Immigration Judge reopened proceedings on a prior occasion after the respondent failed to appear. Following the Immigration Judge’s decision reopening the respondent’s case, a hearing notice scheduling the respondent’s case was mailed to the respondent’s attorney of record, Pablo Cabrera, Esquire. We agree with the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent received proper notice inasmuch as written notice of his hearing was provided to his attorney of record. See section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(A); Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255, 259 (BIA 1985) (finding that notice to an alien’s counsel constitutes notice to the alien); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.26(c)(2); 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a) (providing that whenever a person is required to be given notice, such notice shall be given to the attorney or representative of record, or to the person himself if unrepresented). On the Notice of Appeal, the respondent does not contest the Immigration Judge’s determination that notice was provided to the respondent’s former counsel.
 
To the extent that the respondent argues exceptional circumstances because his attorney did not tell him the date of his hearing after it had been reopened by the Immigration Judge, his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel is untimely as it was not filed within 180 days of the Immigration Judge’s in absentia order. See section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). Moreover, he has not satisfied any of the requirements in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). See also Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1, 3 (A. G. 2009) (directing the Board and Immigration Judges to apply “the Lozada framework” to claims of ineffective assistance of counsel “pending the issuance of a final rule”). See generally Vaz Dos Reis v. Holder, 606 F.3d 1, 5 (1st Cir. 2010) (stating that with respect to motions to reopen in absentia proceedings courts typically treat ineffective assistance of counsel claims as an exceptional circumstance). In sum, we find no reason to disturb the decision of the Immigration Judge.
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order shall be entered:
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Ellen Liebowitz
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 3780656 (BIA)
End of Document

2010 WL 3780637 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EDNA HUNTE-DIAZ
File: AXXX XX5 010 - Los Angeles, CA
September 9, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Anwer Khan
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Remand
 
*1 The respondent, a citizen of Guatemala, was ordered removed from the United States on February 26, 2009. Her appeal of that decision will be dismissed.
 
Under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3), the Board defers to the factual findings of an Immigration Judge, unless they are clearly erroneous, but it retains independent judgment and discretion, subject to applicable governing standards, regarding pure questions of law and the application of a particular standard of law to those facts. Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008). We decide this appeal de novo.
 
The respondent argues in her Notice of Appeal that she was denied consideration for any form of relief from removal on account of the ineffective assistance of her former counsel. She states that she is eligible to be considered for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b. The respondent infers that the Immigration Judge would have considered or even granted her cancellation of removal had it not been for her former attorney’s negligence by not adequately preparing and filing her application. She requests a remand for a hearing.
 
The Board will not grant a request for a remand based on alleged ineffective assistance of counsel unless the respondent first establishes certain requirements. In Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), we held that such motions require: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639. See also Reyes v. Ashcroft, 358 F.3d 592 (9th Cir. 2004); Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518 (9th Cir. 2000).
 
In the instant case, the respondent has not submitted an affidavit or filed a complaint with the bar association against her former attorney. She has also not provided her former attorney with an opportunity to respond to the allegation of ineffective assistance of counsel. Based upon this lack of proof, we are unable to find that the respondent has satisfied the procedural requirements for proving that she was ineffectively represented by her former attorney in these proceedings. We will therefore not remand the record based on this particular allegation.
 
*2 The respondent further vaguely asserts in her Notice of Appeal that the Immigration Judge erred in denying her motion to continue her February 26, 2009, hearing to complete her application for cancellation of removal. The Immigration Judge noted that she had earlier continued the proceeding for that purpose from February 14, 2008, to February 26, 2009. We find for the reasons stated by the Immigration Judge that the decision denying the respondent’s motion to continue was correct. The Immigration Judge’s decision is affirmed.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 3780637 (BIA)
End of Document

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MICKEY H. FLAVIUS
File: AXXX XX1 442 - Guaynabo, PR
September 7, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Paul C. Agu, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jorge Ramos
Senior Attorney
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent appeals the January 28, 2009, decision of the Immigration Judge denying the respondent’s motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review findings of fact by an Immigration Judge under the clearly erroneous standard of review, but we may review de novo questions of law, discretion, and judgment and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii) (2008); see also Matter of V-K-, 24 I&N Dec. 500 (BIA 2008); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
The record reflects that the respondent was granted voluntary departure on September 22, 2008, with an alternate order of removal. On January 16, 2009, the respondent filed a motion to reopen seeking to adjust his status through his United States citizen spouse. The Immigration Judge correctly denied the motion as untimely. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1).
 
In his motion, the respondent alluded to ineffective assistance of counsel by his former counsel. However, the respondent failed to comply with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). In that case, we held that ineffective assistance of counsel claims require: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned is informed of the allegations leveled against him and given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639. The Court of Appeals for the First Circuit, where this case arises, has found the application of the noted requirements to be a necessary condition to obtain a reopening of removal proceeding on the basis of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Belte-Veloz v. Mukasey, 533 F.3d 7, 10 (1st Cir. 2008). Here, the respondent has failed to fulfill any of the above-noted requirements. Nor has the respondent specified how he was prejudiced by his former counsel’s representation. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553, 562 (BIA 2003).
 
*2 On appeal, the respondent maintains that the Immigration Judge should have reopened his proceedings sua sponte. We find that the respondent’s motion does not establish an exceptional situation sufficient to warrant reopening of his removal proceedings. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); see also Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997) (stating that the Board’s power to reopen cases sua sponte is limited to exceptional circumstances and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations).
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered:
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ABDUL HAI
File: AXXX XX5 144 - New York, NY
September 3, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ishrat Sami, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision issued March 11, 2010. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen proceedings in which he was ordered removed in absentia. On appeal, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge erred in denying his motion to reopen because he had established good cause for a continuance of her hearing. The respondent contends that his attorney was required to attend another court hearing at the same time. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). The Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment and all other issues in appeals from decisions of Immigration Judges de novo. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
We find that the Immigration Judge’s decision to proceed with the hearing on December 9, 2009, was appropriate under the circumstances. The regulations provide that an Immigration Judge has the discretion to grant a request for continuance where good cause is shown. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.29. However, parties are obligated to appear for a scheduled hearing unless and until the Immigration Judge expressly grants a request for a continuance. See Matter of Rivera, 19 I&N Dec. 688 (BIA 1988). Since the Immigration Judge did not rule on the respondent’s request for a continuance, the respondent was required to appear for his hearing. See Matter of W-F-, 21 I&N Dec. 503 (BIA 1996) (the filing of a motion for a continuance does not excuse an alien from failing to appear). The Immigration Judge was within her bounds to conduct proceedings in absentia.
 
Finally, we note that the respondent has not made a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel pursuant to Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F. 2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). The respondent has not established any other exceptional circumstance for his failure to appear. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 3780682 (BIA)
End of Document

2010 WL 3536720 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE MARTIN CARRANZA-VARGAS
File: AXXX XX9 796 - Honolulu, HI
August 26, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Manuel Q. Diones, Esquire
*1 After the Ninth Circuit Court of Appeals remanded the case to the Board, we returned the case to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings on July 31, 2009. The Board on May 24, 2010, affirmed the Immigration Judge’s decision, in which the judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen removal proceedings, which were conducted in absentia. The respondent, who is from El Salvador, filed a motion with the Board on August 16, 2010. The motion, to which the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS”) has not responded, will be denied.
 
The Board first considers that the respondent was removed from the United States on August 16, 2010, the same day that the motion was filed. The Ninth Circuit has held that 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(d) cannot be applied to cause the withdrawal of a motion filed by an alien who has been forcibly removed while the motion is pending before the Board. Coyt v. Holder, 593 F.3d 902, 907 (9th Cir. 2010). As the respondent was removed the same day that the motion was filed, the Board will assume that it retains jurisdiction over the motion, despite the respondent’s removal.
 
In the May 24, 2010, decision, we fully considered the record before determining that the respondent received adequate notice of his removal hearing, where he failed to notify the Immigration Court as to his most current mailing address. Section 240(b)(5)(B) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(B). The Board also rejected the respondent’s claim that “exceptional circumstances” excused his failure to appear at the removal hearing. Section 240(b)(5)(C)(ii) of the Act. Although the respondent contended that an attorney, Ronald Oldenburg, had in his possession the respondent’s address, “¿tºhe respondent … indicates his belief that he has not been a victim of ineffective assistance of counsel, and a claim alleging such has not been submitted to the Board.” Board’s May 24, 2010, dec., at 2. Moreover, the record did not contain an affidavit from either the respondent or Oldenburg.
 
The pending motion, in part, reargues the respondent’s contention that the Board should have sustained his appeal of the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny reopening of the in absentia proceedings in this case. As such, the motion is an untimely motion to reconsider, as a motion to reconsider must be filed not later than 30 days after the date of the challenged order. Section 240(c)(6) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b).
 
To the extent that the pending motion may be considered a motion to reopen, it was timely filed. Section 240(c)(7) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). However, a motion to reopen imposes a heavy burden on the moving party to show that reopening is warranted. Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 472-73 (BIA 1992).
 
*2 Here, the motion merely presents an affidavit from the respondent’s girlfriend, stating that she has “instituted an investigation ¿with the Hawaii Office of Disciplinary Counselº with regards to Mr. Oldenburg’s ineffective assistance of counsel leading to ¿the respondent’sº absence from his November 2, 2009, heating.” Kalio Affidavit, at § 6; Respondent’s Mot., at 5, 7-8. The affidavit does not assert that the attorney was ineffective, nor does it explain how the attorney may be responsible for the respondent missing the removal hearing. Moreover, the affidavit does not come from the respondent himself. The girlfriend’s affidavit is insufficient to properly raise a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel against Oldenburg. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003), and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
The respondent’s pending motion will, therefore, be denied.
 
ORDER: The pending motion is denied.
 
David B. Holmes

IN RE: AYFER YALINCAK A.K.A AYFEREFAT YALINCAK A.K.A. IRENE KELLY A.K.A. IRENE CATHERINE MARY KELLY A.K.A. IRENE C. KELLY A.K.A. IRENE KELLY YALINCAK A.K.A. JACKIE A.K.A. AYFEREFAT A.K.A. JACKIE YALINCAK A.K.A. AYFERFAT YALINCAK A.K.A. AYFER ELGEZDI YALINCAK
File: AXXX XX3 720 - Hartford, CT
August 24, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Turkey, moves pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen proceedings and to reissue our last decision, dated May 19, 2009. In that decision, we denied a motion to reopen as untimely. On May 22, 2008, we issued our final order in this matter, dismissing the appeal of the Immigration Judge’s January 16, 2008, decision denying a waiver under section 209(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1159(c), and also denying withholding of removal under section 241(b)(3) of the Act and the Convention Against Torture. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1208.16-18.¿FN1º On July 31, 2008, we denied a motion to reconsider. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b). In removal proceedings, motions to reopen must be filed within 90 days of the final administrative order. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The present motion, filed on April 14, 2010, is untimely as it was not filed within 90 days of our May 22, 2008, final order. 8 C.F.R. § 1002.3(c). However, the respondent argues that this is due to the ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988); see also Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009), vacating 24 I&N Dec. 710 (A.G. 2009). The motion will be denied.
 
The time limit on motions to reopen may be equitably tolled where the alien establishes that the respondent exercised due diligence to discover or act upon the alleged incompetence of her prior attorney in a timely manner. 8 C.F.R. §1003.2(c); Rashid v. Mukasey, 533 F.3d 127 (2nd Cir. 2008); Cekic v. INS, 435 F.3rd 167 (2d Cir. 2006); Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124 (2nd Cir. 2000). In addition, the motion must establish that the ineffective assistance caused the respondent substantial prejudice amounting to a due process violation that would warrant a tolling of the time limit in.. See Id; Romero v. INS, 399 F.3d 109, 112 (2nd Cir. 2005), citing U.S. v. Perez, 330 F.3d 97, 101 (2nd Cir. 2001); Matter of Lozada, supra.
 
*2 The respondent argues essentially that her prior counsel, Attorney Edwin T. Gania, failed to timely file a petition for review with the United States Court of Appeals for the Second Circuit of our May 19, 2009, decision denying the first motion to reopen. Attorney Gania filed petitions for review of our May 22, 2008, merits decision, and of our July 31, 2008, decision denying the motion to reconsider, which were consolidated before the Second Circuit. These petitions were denied. Yalincak v. Holder, 352 Fed.Appx. 584, 2009 WL 3739067 (C.A. 2 November 10, 2009) (unpublished) (petition for rehearing and rehearing en banc denied and mandate issued on May 21, 2010). The respondent claims that she paid Attorney Gania to file a petition for review of our May 19, 2009, decision and he failed to do so, and therefore the decision should be reissued to allow the filing of a timely petition for review.
 
The respondent also argues that Attorney Gania was ineffective by failing to file a full and accurate copy of the “Joint Appendix” (a.k.a. “Joint Appendix”) with the Second Circuit (Motion to Reopen and Motion to Reissue Pursuant to Lozada (“Motion”) at 2, 6). The respondent also argues that there is new factual evidence warranting reopening in discretion pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a).
 
Regarding the first argument, that the Attorney Gania failed to file a petition for review of our May 19, 2009, decision, the motion proffers, inter alia, a February 11, 2010, letter from Attorney Gania to the respondent (Motion at Exh. 3). In the letter, the attorney admitted that he did not file a petition for review of the May 19, 2009, decision, and offered the respondent a settlement. Attorney Gania offered to waive further payment for the Petition for Rehearing and Rehearing En Banc that was pending at the Second Circuit, and to refund the money paid for the motion to reopen. Alternatively, if the respondent decided to pursue a grievance or bar complaint against the attorney’s office, the attorney would be forced to withdraw from the Second Circuit proceeding because of a conflict of interest.
 
The respondent apparently did not file a bar complaint as no evidence of a bar complaint has been proffered. Also, the Petition for Panel Rehearing and Suggestion for Rehearing En Banc (“Petition for Rehearing”) filed at the Second Circuit on February 23, 2010, indicates Attorney Gania still represents the respondent in front of the Second Circuit (Motion at Exh. 8). See also Yalincak v. Holder, supra. Therefore, the respondent has not complied with the procedural requirements to establish an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. See Matter of Lozada, supra. Furthermore, the respondent’s continued retention of the attorney is antithetical to the claim that the attorney provided ineffective assistance. Moreover, as discussed below, no prejudice from any alleged ineffective assistance of counsel has been shown.
 
*3 Regarding the claim that counsel failed to provide a complete copy of the “Joint Appendix” with the Second Circuit, the respondent’s Petition for Rehearing filed with the Second Circuit places the blame for this on the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) (see Motion at Exh. 8 Petition for Rehearing at 4-5). No ineffective assistance of counsel has been argued on this, or any other matter, before the Second Circuit, which belies the respondent’s claim before the Board that she believes Attorney Gania provided ineffective assistance before the Second Circuit, or the Board. The motion does not include arguments that could not have been raised in a timely motion to reopen or in a petition before the Second Circuit that may have warranted a different outcome in the matter. Therefore, the motion does not establish the substantial prejudice amounting to a due process violation that would warrant a tolling of the time limit in these proceedings. See Id; Romero v. INS, supra; Matter of Lozada, supra. The motion thus does not establish an exception to the time limitation on motions to reopen. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c).
 
Finally, the instant motion sets forth discretionary factors for consideration. These include the fact that the respondent’s husband and daughter recently became lawful permanent residents, and that the respondent and her husband are facing health issues (Motion at 16-21). However, there is no objective documentation of the medical or other discretionary factors. Notwithstanding, in consideration of all the factors involved in this matter, this case does not demonstrate an exceptional situation that would warrant the exercise of our discretion to reopen these proceedings or reissue the final decision under our sua sponte authority. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997)(stating that “¿tºhe power to reopen on our own motion is not meant to be used as a general cure for filing defects or to otherwise circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship”). Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen proceedings and to reissue our May 19, 2009, decision is denied.
 
Molly Kendall Clark
FOR THE BOARD

On April 4, 1998, she had been granted asylum (I.J. at 3, 6; Exhs. 1, 2). IN RE: GIL EVANGELISTA SADORRA

August 24, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Chien Yu Michael Wang, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; reconsideration
 
*1 On July 14, 2010, the respondent, who is a native and citizen of the Philippines, filed a motion to reopen these proceedings to afford him the opportunity to apply for adjustment of status. Because the parties are familiar with the facts and procedural history, we do not restate them here except as necessary to explain our decision.
 
The respondent’s motion to reopen will be denied as time and number barred. See sections 240(c)(7)(A) and (C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§1229a(c)(7)(A) and (C)(i) and 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2) (an alien may file one motion to reopen and, with certain exceptions not relevant here, it shall be filed within 90 days of the date of entry of a final administrative order of removal). Our prior order in this case was issued May 2, 2008. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s first motion to reopen on February 5, 2008.
 
The respondent recognizes that his motion is untimely filed and asks us to reopen on our own motion pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). In Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997), we held that the Board’s power to reopen cases sua sponte pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a) is limited to exceptional circumstances and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship.
 
The respondent has failed to present exceptional circumstances which would support sua sponte reopening. Instead, he presents a case where enforcing the time and number limits on motions to reopen will result in hardship to the respondent and his wife. The respondent states that he has been married to his United States citizen wife for six years and she would suffer extreme hardship if he were removed from the United States. See Respondent’s Motion to Reopen, Attachments M, N. The respondent married a United States citizen in April 2004 just after he agreed to voluntarily depart the United States. The acquisition of equities in the United States is to be expected when one continues to remain in the United States, and the respondent’s motion does not demonstrate an “exceptional” situation that would warrant the exercise of our discretion to reopen his proceedings under our sua sponte authority. See Matter of J-J-, supra. Accordingly, the motion to reopen will be denied as time and number barred.
 
The respondent also asks us to reconsider our prior order.¿FN1º The respondent’s motion to reconsider is denied as untimely filed. See section 240(c)(6)(B) of the Act (a motion to reconsider shall be filed within 30 days of the entry of a final administrative order of removal). Additionally, the respondent has not identified any error of law or fact in our prior decision or the Immigration Judge’s decision. See section 240(c)(6)(C) of the Act; 8 C.F.R.§ 1003.2(b). See also Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006). He simply restates his ineffective assistance of counsel claim. We considered the respondent’s arguments before rendering a decision in this case. We decline to revisit them.
 
*2 Based on the foregoing, the motion to reopen and reconsider will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen and reconsider is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD

1

On February 23, 2010, the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit denied the respondent’s petition for review of our May 2, 2008 decision. See Sadorra v. Holder, 367 Fed.Appx. 790, 2010 WL 675246 (9th Cir. 2010).

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MELQUIDO ROJAS
File: AXXX XX2 218 - York, PA
August 24, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ysabel Williams, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Brian G. McDonnell
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(ii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(ii)º - Convicted of two or more crimes involving moral turpitude
 
Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony as defined under section 101(a)(43)(G)
 
Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony as defined under section 101(a)(43)(B)
  
APPLICATION: Remand; continuance
 
The respondent timely appeals the Immigration Judge’s April 20, 2010, decision finding the respondent removable as charged under section 237(a)(2)(A)(iii) of the Immigration and Nationality Act (“Act”), 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii), and denying his motion for a continuance. The respondent argues on appeal that the Immigration Judge erred in denying his continuance request. We note that the respondent does not contest the Immigration Judge’s finding of removability and does not assert that he is eligible for any forms of relief. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) argues that the Immigration Judge’s decision is correct and should be summarily affirmed. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent claims that he received ineffective assistance of counsel from the defense attorney who represented him during his criminal proceedings in Pennsylvania. Specifically, the respondent claims that his guilty plea was not voluntary because he was not properly advised by his lawyer that a conviction could lead to adverse immigration consequences. As the respondent notes, the United States Supreme Court has held that a criminal lawyer representing a non-citizen in connection with a guilty plea has a constitutional duty to advise the defendant that conviction may pose a risk of deportation. Padilla v. Kentucky, 130 S. Ct. 1473 (2010). In light of Padilla, the respondent claims that the Immigration Judge violated his right to due process of law by failing to continue his removal proceedings while he pursued a collateral attack on his conviction in the New York courts.
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent did not state “good cause” for a continuance. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29, 1240.6 (2010). A collateral attack does not impair the validity of an alien’s conviction for immigration purposes unless and until the conviction is overturned. E.g., Montilla v. INS, 926 F.2d 162, 164 (2d Cir. 1991); Matter of Madrigal, 21 I&N Dec. 323, 327 (BIA 1996). While there may be some truly exceptional cases in which an Immigration Judge might justifiably grant a continuance during the pendency of a criminal matter, we reject the respondent’s assertion that he has a due process right to such a continuance. An Immigration Judge presented with a facially valid removal charge is not required by principles of fundamental fairness to adjourn removal proceedings every time an alien raises the speculative possibility that a collateral criminal proceeding might eventually affect the immigration case. Requiring a continuance in such contexts would invite abuse, and would often unnecessarily prolong the mandatory detention, at Government expense, of aliens with criminal convictions. See section 236(c) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1226(c) (requiring the DHS to detain most criminal aliens, without bond, pending the conclusion of their removal proceedings). Furthermore, it has been over 3 months since the Immigration Judge rendered his decision, yet we have received no indication that the respondent’s conviction has been vacated or materially modified in any way by the Pennsylvania courts. Under the circumstances, we have no basis for concluding that the respondent suffered substantial prejudice arising from the Immigration Judge’s denial of his continuance request.
 
*2 In conclusion, we find no error or due process violation in the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s continuance request and ordering him removed. In the event the respondent ultimately prevails on his collateral challenge, he shall of course be free to file a motion to reopen the proceedings. Section 240(c)(7) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7). The following order shall be issued.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Patricia A. Cole
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 3536704 (BIA)
End of Document

2010 WL 3536677 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GLADYS OMARI-AGYAPONG
File: AXXX XX6 732 - Baltimore, MD
August 18, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Sopo Ngwa, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has filed a second motion to reopen to rescind the in absentia order of removal that was entered against her on January 23, 2008. She does not address the fact that her motion is untimely and number-barred, but urges that reopening should be granted because she was the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel. Essentially, she urges that she failed to receive notice of the scheduled hearing in this case because her prior attorney never filed an appearance form with the Immigration Court or a notification of her change of address, and the notice of hearing was therefore served at her old address. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has not responded to the current motion.
 
Having reviewed the respondent’s submissions, and in view of the unique circumstances of this case and the lack of opposition by the DHS, we find it appropriate to reopen these proceedings sua sponte and remand this matter to the Immigration Court for a new hearing. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). There is no dispute in this case that the respondent did not actually receive the notice of hearing mailed by the Immigration Court on October 4, 2007, as it was returned by the United States Postal Service with the notation “Return to Sender.” While it is not clear whether the respondent’s prior attorney agreed to represent her with regard to her removal proceedings, she has presented persuasive evidence that she reasonably believed he was representing her both before the USCIS and the Immigration Court and that she believed he had filed an appearance on her behalf and had reported her October 2006 change of address to the Immigration Court. We are convinced of an exceptional situation that warrants reopening of these removal proceedings. Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). Accordingly, we will reopen these proceedings sua sponte, vacate the in absentia removal order, and remand this matter for further proceedings.
 
ORDER: The removal proceedings are reopened sua sponte, and the Immigration Judge’s January 23, 2008, in absentia order of removal, is vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD

IN RE: JUAN CARLOS VALERO-MONTOYA A.K.A. JOSE A. ARCE
File: AXXX XX9 990 - New York, NY
August 11, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Louis Leeder, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Colombia, appeals from an Immigration Judge’s decision dated August 18, 2009, denying his motion to reopen proceedings. The respondent was ordered removed in absentia on January 19, 2000. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
Upon de novo review, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision for the reasons stated therein. In reaching this conclusion we note that the respondent’s assertion that he received inaccurate advice from an “agency” and an unidentified attorney which scared him to the point he decided not to go to his hearing on January 19, 2000, is not an exceptional circumstance to excuse his failure to appear. See section 240(e)(1) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(e)(1); 8 U.S.C. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). Although the respondent has essentially alleged that he received ineffective assistance of counsel from the unidentified attorney, he has failed to comply with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Moreover, he has claimed that he married a United States citizen and that she has filed an I-130 visa petition on his behalf. However, he failed to present evidence that the I-130 had been filed and approved or otherwise establish that he is eligible for adjustment of status. Consequently, we find no basis to disturb the Immigration Judge’s determination.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Frederick D. Hess

2010 WL 3355073 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: IVAN ERIC LINTON
File: AXXX XX8 528 - Miami, FL
August 10, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Richard Jurgens
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The Board entered the final administrative order in this case on August 29, 2008. This case was last before the Board on May 25, 2010, however, when we denied the respondent’s motion to reopen and reconsider for having been filed out of time. In that decision, we found that the respondent had not properly set forth a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, or otherwise offered arguments or evidence of any exceptional situation to persuade us to reconsider our prior order sua sponte or to reopen the proceedings on our own motion under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). On June 23, 2010, the respondent filed the instant motion. The Department of Homeland Security opposes the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
Although the motion is titled “Motion for Reconsideration”, the respondent has not contested the bases of our decision denying his previous motion. See generally Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006) (holding that a motion to reconsider a decision of the Board must include, inter alia, an allegation of material factual or legal errors in the prior decision that is supported by pertinent authority). Instead, the respondent has submitted new evidence, including evidence that since our prior order he has attempted to comply with the procedural requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim before the Board. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). We find, therefore, that the instant motion is in the nature of a motion to reopen. See Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991). As a motion to reopen, the motion is untimely and number-barred. See sections 240(c)(7)(A), (C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229a(c)(7)(A), (C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). We do not find that any of the general exceptions to the filing restrictions apply to the respondent’s motion. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(3). Moreover, for the reasons below, we do not find that the exercise of our sua sponte authority is warranted in this case. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a).
 
In our decision dismissing the appeal, we found no legal error in the Immigration Judge’s decision pretermitting the respondent’s application for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(c) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1158(c), because the 1989 conviction underlying the charges of removability was by jury trial. See I.N.S. v. St. Cyr, 533 U.S. 289 (U.S. 2001); Alexandre v. United States Attorney General, 452 F.3d 1204 (11th Cir. 2006). The respondent now appears to blame his former counsel for his inability to apply for the section 212(c) waiver. Specifically, he asserts that his former counsel failed to inform him of the immigration consequences of his criminal convictions when she conceded removability on his behalf. See Respondent’s Motion at 9.
 
*2 It was due to the respondent’s criminal convictions, rather than to any action on the part of his former counsel in these proceedings, that the respondent was placed in these removal proceedings. The respondent has not contested the fact that he was convicted by a jury trial rather than by a plea of guilty. Cf Padilla v. Kentucky, 130 S.Ct. 1473 (U.S. 2010) (holding that counsel engaged in deficient performance by failing to advise the defendant that his plea of guilty made him subject to automatic deportation). Nor is there any evidence offered in support of this motion to indicate that an attack on the respondent’s convictions has resulted in a vacatur. See Matter of Ponce de Leon, 21 I&N Dec. 154 (BIA 1996). We find, therefore, that the respondent has failed to establish that his former counsel committed any error resulting in prejudice to the respondent when she conceded removability on his behalf. See Dakane v. United States Attorney General, 371 F.3d 771 (11th Cir. 2004). In sum, we reject the respondent’s ineffective assistance of counsel claim, and we conclude that reopening sua sponte is not warranted in this case. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). Accordingly, the respondent’s untimely, number-barred motion to reopen will be denied.¿FN1º
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied
 
Frederick D. Hess

The respondent’s request for a stay of removal is denied.

2010 WL 3355059 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SUNIL SOODOO A.K.A. SUNIL RAVIE A.K.A. RAUIE SOODOO
File: AXXX XX3 853 - New York, NY
August 5, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 On April 22, 2010, the respondent, a native and citizen of Guyana, filed a timely motion to reopen these removal proceedings to afford him the opportunity to apply for adjustment of status and a waiver of inadmissibility pursuant to former section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c).
 
In our prior order we agreed with the Immigration Judge that it was futile for the respondent to apply for a 212(c) waiver because it could not waive the respondent’s 2005 conviction for the unlawful possession of more than 30 grams of marijuana.
 
The respondent now argues that his accredited representative erred in admitting that he was convicted of the offense of unlawful possession of marijuana in 2005 and conceding that he was removable under section 237(a)(2)(B)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i), as an alien convicted of a controlled substance violation (Exhs. 4, 9 and 13; Tr. at 72-73). He claims that his accredited representative should not have introduced the lab report which reflected that the respondent was in possession of 3.8 ounces of marijuana. See Respondent’s Motion at 3. The respondent asserts that he was prejudiced when the lab report, which does not constitute a record of conviction, was submitted to the Immigration Court. In short, the respondent asserts that the Department of Homeland Security was only able to establish that he was convicted of possessing more than 30 grams of marijuana and that he is removable pursuant to section 237(a)(2)(B)(i) of the Act - because of the actions of his representative. The respondent has complied with the requirements for raising an ineffective assistance of counsel claim set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). See also Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009), vacating 24 I&N Dec. 710 (A.G. 2009).
 
Essentially, the respondent seeks to withdraw the admission and concession entered by his accredited representative. See Respondent’s Motion, Attachment C. We have long held that absent egregious circumstances, a distinct and formal admission made before, during, or even after a proceeding by an attorney acting in his professional capacity binds his client as a judicial admission; and that there is a strong presumption that such a decision is a reasonable tactical decision. See Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377 (BIA 1986). Notably, the respondent does not claim that the pleading is factually untrue or incorrect. Given the facts and circumstances of this case, we find that the respondent is bound by the admissions and concessions he made by and through his representative.
 
*2 Additionally, the respondent’s marijuana conviction would have rendered him inadmissible pursuant to section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(II), and, thus, ineligible for adjustment of status. See section 245(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(a). Even if the lab report had not been submitted, he would be ineligible for a corresponding waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(h) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(h), because he is a lawful permanent resident who has been convicted of an aggravated felony.
 
Based on the foregoing, the respondent’s motion to reopen will be denied. See generally Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 472 (BIA 1992) (motion to reopen may be denied based upon the failure to establish a prima facie case for the relief sought).
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD

2010 WL 3355096 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: PEDRO ALONSO BONILLA-VIDAL A.K.A. PEDRO ALONSO BONILLA
File: AXXX XX6 987 - Houston, TX
August 5, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Sergio A. Saldivar, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Alice Hogue Kay
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on April 2, 2002, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision finding the respondent ineligible for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a). We also denied the respondent’s motion to remand the record. On July 21, 2010, the respondent filed a motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has filed an opposition to the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
With certain exceptions, a motion to reopen must be filed not later than 90 days after the final administrative order of removal. See section 240(c)(7) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The respondent’s motion was filed more than 8 years after the final administrative order of removal and is therefore untimely. Furthermore, the respondent has not established an exceptional situation warranting our consideration of his motion sua sponte. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).
 
The respondent contends that reopening is warranted in light of the decisions of the United States Supreme Court in Carachuri-Rosendo v. Holder, 130 S. Ct. 2577 (2010) and in Padilla v. Kentucky, 130 S. Ct. 1473 (2010). The respondent asserts that he would contest his charge of removability based on multiple drug possession convictions under the holding in Carachuri-Rosendo v. Holder, supra, and that he “would show through testimony and evidence presented that he was not properly admonished of the possibility of immigration consequences following his pleas to these possession charges as now required in the case of Padilla v. Kentucky,” supra (Mot. at 5).
 
The DHS correctly contends that the respondent has not shown how Carachuri-Rosendo v. Holder, supra, would change the outcome of his case. In Carachuri, the Supreme Court held that when an alien has been convicted of a simple possession offense that has not been enhanced based on the fact of a prior conviction, he has not been convicted of a felony punishable as such under the Controlled Substances Act, 18 U.S.C. § 924(c)(2), and therefore has not been convicted of an “aggravated felony” for purposes of section 240A(a)(3) of the Act. The respondent was not found subject to removal based on an aggravated felony conviction. He was found subject to removal under section 237(a)(2)(B)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i) (conviction for offense relating to a controlled substance other than a single offense involving possession for one’s own use of thirty grams or less of marijuana).
 
*2 Also, the respondent has not shown that his case should be reopened under Padilla v. Kentucky, supra. In Padilla, the Supreme Court held that competent legal advice includes notice to aliens in criminal proceedings of the potential effects of those proceedings on their immigration status or benefits. The respondent has not presented evidence with his motion to support the claim that he received ineffective assistance of counsel in his criminal cases. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1) (motion to reopen must be supported by affidavits and other evidentiary material). In any event and more fundamentally, the respondent does not assert that either of his convictions has been vacated, and based on the record before us, his convictions are valid for immigration purposes.
 
The respondent’s motion is untimely and does not present an exceptional situation that warrants reopening sua sponte. Accordingly the motion will be denied. The motion for a stay of removal pending adjudication of this motion is moot.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 3355096 (BIA)
