2010 WL 2390777 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARK LERONA DE LA CRUZ
File: AXXX XX3 603 - York, PA
May 25, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Christopher B. Jones, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jeffrey T. Bubier
Senior Attorney
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a citizen of the Philippines, was ordered removed from the United States on March 12, 2008. He filed a motion to reopen on January 19, 2010. The Immigration Judge denied it on January 28, 2010, and this appeal followed. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
Under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3), the Board defers to the factual findings of an Immigration Judge, unless they are clearly erroneous, but it retains independent judgment and discretion, subject to applicable governing standards, regarding pure questions of law and the application of a particular standard of law to those facts. Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008). We decide this appeal de novo.
 
The record reflects that the respondent’s motion to reopen was filed with the Immigration Judge more than 90 days after the date in which the final administrative decision by the Immigration Judge was rendered. The motion is therefore untimely. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1); Matter of Susma, 22 I&N Dec. 947 (BIA 1999) (holding that a motion to reopen must be filed within 90 days after the date of the final administrative decision of the Board or Immigration Judge).
 
The respondent argues on appeal that he was ineffectively represented at his hearing, and the Immigration Judge should have reopened these proceedings based on ineffective assistance of counsel. However, the respondent has not established that his former attorney’s negligence prevented him from filing his motion to reopen in a timely manner. We therefore will not equitably toll the 90-day period to seek reopening even assuming that the respondent was a victim of ineffective assistance of counsel at his hearing. See Mahmood v. Gonzales, 427 F.3d 248, 252-53 (3d Cir. 2005) (any equitable tolling of the motions deadline is unavailable when a party fails to exercise due diligence on his own behalf).
 
The respondent further argues on appeal that he was ineffectively represented by his criminal attorney who did not advise him of the immigration consequences of his plea agreement. The respondent has failed to introduce any evidence that his conviction is no longer valid. The Board is not empowered to consider the respondent’s constitutional challenges to the validity of his conviction. We consider the conviction final until a criminal court enters a decision vacating it. See generally Matter of Polanco, 20 I&N Dec. 894 (BIA 1994).
 
*2 The respondent additionally argues on appeal that the Immigration Judge erred in denying his motion because the Department of Homeland Security has unduly delayed his removal. The Board has no jurisdiction to require the swift removal of the respondent. We further have no authority to reopen these proceedings on account of the alleged undue delay in the respondent’s removal from the United States. We do not find that the respondent has established that reopening of these proceedings is appropriate based on a denial to him of due process. We further will not grant the respondent’s untimely motion to reopen based on his assertion that denying the motion will likely cause both him and his family hardship. The Immigration Judge’s decision is affirmed.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 2390777 (BIA)
End of Document

2010 WL 2390771 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MIGUEL GUTIERREZ-OCHOA
File: AXXX XX9 049 - Buffalo, NY
May 24, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
L. Manuel Macias, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Robert P. Levy
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 This case was last before the Board on June 9, 2009, when we determined that the respondent’s interlocutory appeal became moot upon the Immigration Judge’s decision of May 1, 2009, ordering the respondent removed in absentia. The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision dated June 24, 2009, denying his motion to reopen. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
On appeal the respondent asserts that the Immigration Judge erred in characterizing his claim as one of ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent claims that his attorney, Mr. L. Manuel Macias, previously filed an appeal with the Board regarding the Immigration Judge’s denial of his motion to change venue, and Mr. Macias erroneously changed his internal records to indicate that the respondent had no upcoming hearings before the Immigration Court. Mr. Macias claims that the administrative error was his fault and not the fault of the respondent, such that reopening is warranted based on exceptional circumstances, or that the case should be reopened sua sponte.
 
An order issued following proceedings conducted in absentia pursuant to section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(A), may be rescinded only upon a motion to reopen which demonstrates that the alien failed to appear because of exceptional circumstances, because he did not receive proper notice of the hearing, or because he was in Federal or State custody and failed to appear through no fault of his own. Section 240(b)(5)(C), 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C). The term “exceptional circumstances” refers to exceptional circumstances beyond the control of the alien, such as serious illness of the alien or death of an immediate relative, but not including less compelling circumstances. Section 240(e)(1), 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(e)(1).
 
As the respondent’s attorney received notice of the hearing, proper notice was provided to the respondent. See Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985) (holding that notice to an alien’s counsel constitutes notice to the alien); 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a). The respondent did not establish that he failed to appear for the hearing due to “exceptional circumstances,” as defined in section 240(e)(1) of the Act. Furthermore, the Board has held that the mere filing of a motion for a change of venue does not relieve the respondent of his responsibility to appear, and unless the Immigration Judge grants the motion, the respondent remains obligated to appear at the appointed date and time. Matter of Rivera, 19 I&N Dec. 688 (BIA 1988); Matter of Patel, 19 I&N Dec. 260 (BIA 1985), aff’d, Patel v. INS, 803 F.2d 804 (5th Cir. 1986).
 
*2 We have held that ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an “exceptional circumstance” for purposes of rescission of an in absentia order where the alien establishes that the failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel and where the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), are satisfied. See Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec.472 (BIA 1996); see also Matter of Rivera, 21 I&N Dec. 599 (BIA 1996). In a decision dated June 3, 2009, the Attorney General determined that the Board and Immigration Judges should apply the pre-Compean standards to all pending and future motions to reopen based upon ineffective assistance of counsel, regardless of when such motions were filed. See Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1, 3 (A.G. 2009). Consequently, in his decision of June 24, 2009, the Immigration Judge correctly applied Lozada when he adjudicated the respondent’s motion to reopen.
 
An attorney’s administrative error or his failure to advise a client that a change of venue motion had not been granted and that he must appear for the hearing is not a basis for reopening absent compliance with Lozada. We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not established ineffective assistance of counsel for his failure to appear. The respondent has not submitted an affidavit asserting that he failed to appear due to ineffective assistance of counsel, nor did he indicate that a disciplinary complaint had been filed against his attorney. The Immigration Judge correctly determined that reopening is not warranted in this matter, as the respondent has made no attempt to comply with the requirements set forth in Lozada.
 
Finally, the respondent requests that the Board exercise its sua sponte authority to reopen his removal proceedings. We find that the facts presented in this case do not warrant an intervention of our sua sponte authority to reopen the proceedings. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); see also Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 2390771 (BIA)
End of Document

IN RE: JOSEPH KOMENAN A.K.A. ABDOULIE SAMBA

May 24, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Laine S. Posel, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; reopening
 
*1 The respondent has filed a motion to reconsider the Board’s October 13, 2009, decision dismissing his appeal from the Immigration Judge’s denial of his motion to reconsider. The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion.
 
At issue in this case is the respondent’s claim that he failed to attend his scheduled hearing on July 11, 2008, because his initial attorney in this matter, Michael Wallace, told him the wrong date of the hearing and did not give him a copy of the hearing notice that was personally served on counsel while the respondent was outside of the courtroom. With his current motion, the respondent has presented new evidence, in the form of an affidavit from attorney Wallace admitting his mistake in telling the respondent the wrong date of the hearing and in not providing him with a copy of the hearing notice. The respondent has also explained why he has not filed a complaint with the Georgia State Bar. The respondent previously presented evidence that, at the time of his scheduled hearing, he was married to a United States citizen and they had a child who was born in September 2007, and he thus had every reason to attend his hearing.
 
Although the motion is untimely and number-barred, and the United States Court of Appeals for the Eleventh Circuit does not recognize equitable tolling of the time limits for claims of ineffective assistance of counsel, we will nevertheless exercise our discretion to reopen these proceedings sua sponte under the unique circumstances of this case, especially given that the DHS has stated no opposition to the motion. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); see also Ali Abdi v. U.S. Attorney General, 430 F.3d 1148, 1150 (11th Cir. 2005). Accordingly, these proceedings will be reopened, the in absentia order will be vacated, and the record will be remanded for further proceedings.
 
ORDER: These removal proceedings are reopened sua sponte, and the Immigration Judge’s in absentia order entered on July 11, 2008, is vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion, and for the entry of a new decision.
 
Frederick D. Hess

IN RE: RISTO PETKOV

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on October 2, 2009, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal and reinstated a period of 60 days’ voluntary departure. On November 3, 2009, the respondent filed a timely motion to reopen. The filing of his motion to reopen within the reinstated voluntary departure period automatically terminates the prior grant of voluntary departure. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(e)(1) & (2). The previously-entered voluntary departure order will therefore be vacated. The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion.
 
Applicable regulations provide that a motion to reopen “shall state the new facts that will be proven at a hearing to be held if the motion is granted and shall be supported by affidavits or other evidentiary material.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). The respondent urges in his motion that certain evidence pertaining to his father was not submitted because he was advised against it by his attorney, and that there were some deficiencies in the translation of his testimony at the hearing. The respondent, however, despite stating that he would submit evidence later to supplement his motion, has submitted no affidavits or other evidentiary material in support of his motion to reopen. Nor has he identified any particular translation errors or shown how they affected his case. Moreover, we point out that his vague claim of ineffective assistance of counsel does not meet any of the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), such that he has properly raised such a claim. Accordingly, the motion to reopen will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The Board’s October 2, 2009, order reinstating voluntary departure is vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The Immigration Judge’s alternate order of removal to Macedonia is now in effect.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD

    1  Order Vacated on Reconsideration by IN RE: RAMIRO A. POLANCO URENA A.K.A. POLANCO RAMIRO A.K.A. EDY RODIGUEZ, BIA, July 16, 2010

*1 The final administrative decision was issued by the Board on April 26, 2010, and the Board subsequently granted a stay of removal. 2010 WL 2224505 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ROLANDO MALIG
File: AXXX XX9 731 - Seattle, WA
May 20, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
David Sturman, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Robert F. Peck
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(A), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(A)º - Inadmissible at time of entry or adjustment of status under section 212(a)(6)(C)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(C)(i)º - Fraud or willful misrepresentation of a material fact
 
Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of the Philippines, has appealed the Immigration Judge’s March 31, 2009, decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen an order of removal entered in absentia on March 9, 2006.
 
The respondent argues that the Immigration Judge erred in denying the motion based on an ineffective assistance of counsel argument because the respondent actually argued in the motion that he had not received proper notice. We agree. The Immigration Judge did not address the respondent’s notice argument at all. In order for the parties to fairly contest the Immigration Judge’s determination on appeal and for this Board to meaningfully review the arguments on appeal, the Immigration Judge must identify and fully explain the reasons for his decision. Matter of M-P-, 20 I&N Dec. 786 (BIA 1994). This includes an analysis of the relevant arguments being made by the parties. Accordingly, the record will be remanded for consideration of the respondent’s arguments for reopening the in absentia order.
 
Upon remand, the Immigration Judge shall consider the respondent’s arguments pursuant to the latest relevant precedent, allow the parties an opportunity to submit evidence relevant to such an analysis, and issue a new decision. See, e.g., Matter of M-R-A-, 24 I&N Dec. 665, 674-76 (BIA 2008) (setting forth the standards for determining whether a respondent has presented sufficient evidence to overcome the weaker presumption of delivery that attaches to notices sent by regular mail); Matter of C-R-C-, 24 I&N Dec. 677 (BIA 2008) (holding that the respondent overcame the presumption of delivery of a Notice to Appear sent by regular mail where he submitted an affidavit stating that he did not receive the notice, submitted additional evidence indicating he had an incentive to appear, and exercised due diligence in promptly obtaining counsel and requesting reopening of the proceedings); see also Sembiring v. Gonzales, 499 F.3d 981 (9th Cir. 2007) (discussing weaker presumption of delivery of hearing notice sent through regular mail and holding that an unsworn written statement of non-receipt of the notice may be sufficient to rebut the presumption of delivery).¿FN1º
 
*2 ORDER: The record is remanded for further proceedings in accordance with this decision.
 
Patricia A. Cole
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

We understand the respondent to be making a lack of notice argument, not an ineffective assistance of counsel argument. If, however, the respondent makes an ineffective assistance of counsel argument on remand, such a claim must be evaluated under the most recent legal standards. See Matter of Compean Bangaly & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009) (vacating Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 24 I&N Dec. 710 (A.G. 2009), and pending the outcome of a rulemaking process, directing the Board and the Immigration Judges to continue to apply the previously established standards for reviewing motions to reopen based on claims of ineffective assistance of counsel).

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MAURICIO ERNESTO MORALES-GARCIA
File: AXXX XX1 812 - Los Angeles, CA
May 20, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Brenda C. Diaz, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a citizen of Guatemala, was ordered removed from the United States in absentia on September 9, 2002, after failing to appear at a hearing. He filed a motion to reopen on December 29, 2009. The Immigration Judge denied it on February 1, 2010, and this appeal followed. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
Under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3), the Board defers to the factual findings of an Immigration Judge, unless they are clearly erroneous, but it retains independent judgment and discretion, subject to applicable governing standards, regarding pure questions of law and the application of a particular standard of law to those facts. Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008). We decide the issues on appeal de novo.
 
The respondent acknowledges on appeal that the Notice of Hearing was mailed to 6141 Hazelton Avenue, Van Nuys, California 91401, the address reflected on his June 18, 2002, motion to change venue. According to the respondent, his former counsel provided an incorrect address therein on his behalf. The respondent denies having received notice of his hearing.
 
The alleged failure of a counsel to provide the Immigration Court with the respondent’s correct address would appear to constitute ineffective assistance of counsel, and it perhaps would be an “exceptional circumstance” warranting reopening of these proceedings. However, the respondent must have filed his motion to reopen based on “exceptional circumstances” within 180 days of the order of removal. See section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C). Because a request for reopening based on “exceptional circumstances” was not made within 180 days of the date of the respondent’s order of removal, it is barred from consideration. The respondent has also not satisfied any of the procedural requirements for asserting a claim for reopening based on ineffective assistance of counsel. See De Zavala v. Ashcroft, 385 F.3d 879, 883 (5th Cir. 2004); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 638 (BIA 1988).
 
We do not find that a failure of notice occurred herein. The respondent is not entitled to receive notice of his hearing on account of the failure to provide the Immigration Court with a correct mailing address. See section 240(b)(5)(B) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(5)(B).
 
The respondent further argues on appeal that the Notice to Appear is not genuine or authentic. We have reviewed the document, and it appears on its face to contain all the indicia of reliability. It contains the factual allegations giving rise to a charge of removability, is issued and signed by an authorized individual, and it is personally served on the respondent. The respondent has not identified any aspect of the Notice to Appear that would suggest that it is not a genuine document issued by the Department of Homeland Security (DHS). The DHS is not required, as argued on appeal, to establish the chain of custody of the Notice to Appear. The Immigration Judge’s decision is affirmed.
 
*2 Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Charles K. Adkins-Blanch
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 2224504 (BIA)

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ORLANDO MEDINA LOPEZ A.K.A. ORLANDO MEDINA
File: AXXX XX4 570 - York, PA
May 20, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jeffrey T. Bubier
Senior Attorney
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i)º - Convicted of controlled substance violation
 
Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony
  
APPLICATION: Removability; remand
 
The respondent appeals from the Immigration Judge’s February 17, 2010, decision finding him removable as charged and ordering him removed to the Dominican Republic. The Department of Homeland Security seeks a summary affirmance. The respondent’s request for oral argument is denied. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e). The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008); Matter of V-K-, 24 I&N Dec. 500 (BIA 2008).
 
We adopt and affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872 (BIA 1994). We agree with the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent’s 2001 conviction for the Attempted Criminal Sale of a Controlled Substance (cocaine) in violation of section 110-220.39 of the New York State Penal Law qualifies as an aggravated felony. Section 101(a)(43)(B) of the Immigration and Nationality Act (Act), 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(B); Evanson v. U.S. Att’y General, 550 F.3d 284, 288 (3d Cir. 2008).
 
The respondent seeks a remand based upon his claim that his criminal case has been reopened, and thus his drug conviction is not final for immigration purposes (Respondent’s Appeal Brief at 4-6). The respondent has submitted new evidence on appeal, including a letter from the Supreme Court of New York County to support his claim. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). The new evidence does not indicate that the respondent’s 2001 conviction has been overturned, vacated, or expunged; rather, the evidence merely indicates that the respondent’s post judgment motion has been scheduled before a judge. Therefore, the respondent’s reliance upon Pinho v. Gonzales, 432 F.3d 193 (3d Cir. 2005), is misplaced. The respondent also argues on appeal that his guilty plea was involuntary, and he was not advised of his constitutional rights at the time of his guilty plea (Respondent’s Appeal Brief at 6); however, there is no objective evidence in the record to support the respondent’s assertions.
 
*2 The respondent’s arguments are in the nature of collateral attacks on his conviction; however, neither the Immigration Judge nor this Board can entertain a collateral attack on a judgment of conviction unless that judgment is void on its face, and cannot go behind the judicial record to determine the guilt or innocence of the respondent. Matter of Madrigal-Calvo, 21 I&N Dec. 323, 327 (BIA 1996); Matter of Rodriguez-Carrillo, 22 I&N Dec. 1031, 1034 (BIA 1999).
 
The respondent also alleges ineffective assistance of counsel, yet he has not complied with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). See Mudric v. U.S. Att’y General, 469 F.3d 94, 100 (3d Cir. 2006) (affirming the Board’s denial of a motion to reopen for failure to comply with the procedural requirements of Lozada). Furthermore, there is no objective evidence in the record to indicate that the respondent was misadvised regarding the potential immigration consequences of his guilty plea. See Padilla v. Kentucky, 130 S.Ct. 1473 (2010). Accordingly, based upon the evidence in the record, we conclude that the respondent’s conviction is final for immigration purposes and a remand is not warranted.¿FN1º 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1); Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992). In view of the foregoing, the following order shall be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

We deny the respondent’s April 5, 2010, motion and note that the record contains all of the transcripts pertaining to the respondent. Additionally, in his April 12, 2010, appeal brief the respondent does not assert that the transcripts are missing or unavailable or that he was unable to prepare his appeal brief due to missing transcripts.

IN RE: DAVID ROJAS

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS

Daniel Thomann, Esquire

Daniel Rah
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony as defined in section 101(a)(43)(B), I&N Act ¿ 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(B)º (not sustained)
 
Sec. 237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i)º - Convicted of controlled substance violation (not sustained)
 
Lodged: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony as defined in section 101(a)(43)(T), I&N Act ¿ 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(T)º (not sustained)
  
APPLICATION: Termination of proceedings
 
The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) appeals the Immigration Judge’s December 10, 2009, decision terminating removal proceedings based on the DHS’s failure to sustain the removability charges noticed in the Notice to Appear.¿FN1º The respondent has filed an opposition. We will remand the record for further proceedings.
 
We review findings of fact made by the Immigration Judge under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i); Matter of R-S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 629, 637 (BIA 2003). We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
The respondent is a native and citizen of Mexico who adjusted to lawful permanent resident status on July 22, 2003. Section 245 of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255. On August 18, 2008, the respondent pled no contest and was convicted of one count of possession with intent to manufacture, distribute or deliver 200 grams or less of tetrahydrocannabinols in violation of WISC. STAT. ANN. § 961.41(lm)(h)(1) (West 2009). The respondent was served with a Notice to Appear on June 25, 2009, that cited the respondent’s August 18, 2008, conviction in the factual allegations, and charged him as removable on the basis of an aggravated felony conviction as defined in section 101(a)(43)(B) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. 1101(a)(43)(B), and a controlled substance conviction. Section 237(a)(2)(A)(iii) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii); Section 237(a)(2)(B)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i).
 
*2 On September 23, 2009, the respondent filed a motion to withdraw his no contest plea with the Circuit Court for Winnebago County pursuant to WISC. STAT. ANN. § 971.08(2) (West 2009), asserting that he did not appreciate that his no contest plea could result in deportation, and that because he had lived here many years and his family remained here, withdrawal of his plea was warranted in the interest of justice. The respondent’s motion did not assert that he failed to receive the statutory advisals required by § 971.08(2), or that he received ineffective assistance of counsel. On October 23, 2009, the circuit court granted the respondent’s motion, vacated his August 18, 2008, conviction, and amended it to one count of felony bail jumping in violation of WISC. STAT. ANN. § 946.49(1)(b) (West 2009). The respondent was sentenced to 2 years of probation.
 
The minutes of the circuit court’s October 23, 2009, hearing reflect that in granting the respondent’s motion, the court did not dispute the fact that the respondent did not understand that his no contest plea would effect deportation. Nothing in the minutes identifies the statutory basis on which the circuit court granted the motion to withdraw, or any other statutory or factual basis for granting the motion.
 
The DHS bears the burden to prove that an alien’s conviction has been vacated solely to alleviate immigration hardship, and thus remains valid for establishing removability in immigration proceedings. Sandoval v. INS, 240 F.3d 577, 581 (7th Cir. 2001). However, we will remand the record because the Immigration Judge’s factual finding that the vacatur was due to a “constitutional” defect in the respondent’s plea is not supported by the record (I.J. at 4). Nothing in the record indicates that the circuit court vacated the respondent’s conviction due to a constitutional defect. The minutes of the October 23, 2009, hearing reflect that the court did not dispute the fact that the respondent did not appreciate the immigration consequences of his plea (Exh. 5). That does not mean, however, that the court attributed the respondent’s misunderstanding to a constitutional or procedural defect underlying the plea, or that any such defect was the legal basis for the vacatur.
 
The respondent’s motion to withdraw also fails to shed sufficient light on the basis for the vacatur, as the motion raises a constitutional argument and an immigration hardship argument, and we cannot discern from this record which argument, if either, the circuit court relied upon to vacate the conviction. The respondent’s motion contends that his failure to understand the immigration consequences of his plea meant that his plea was not knowing and voluntary. The respondent’s motion also asserts that the interests of justice warrant allowing him to withdraw his plea, as he has lived in the United States for almost his entire life and his entire family resides here as well. The October 23, 2009, hearing minutes do not reflect whether the circuit court’s vacatur was in response to this request from the respondent, which is clearly a request to alleviate the immigration hardships arising from the respondent’s August 18, 2008, conviction.
 
*3 Because we have limited fact-finding ability on appeal, we will remand the record for the Immigration Judge to reassess the facts and decide whether the DHS has met its burden of proving that the circuit court’s vacatur was solely to alleviate the respondent’s expressed immigration hardships. Contrary to the DHS’s argument, our holding in Matter of Chavez-Martinez, 24 I&N Dec. 272 (BIA 2007), at 273, does not apply here. In Matter of Chavez-Martinez, supra, at 273, we held that the respondent had the burden of proving the basis for a vacatur when the issue was raised in the context of the respondent’s motion to reopen. Matter of Chavez-Martinez, supra, at 273. DHS Brief at 8-9. At that “late stage of the proceedings,” the burden was appropriately placed on the respondent. Matter of Chavez-Martinez, supra, at 273.
 
On remand, the parties shall have an opportunity to provide additional evidence regarding the circumstances of the vacatur, including any transcripts of the plea hearing and the October 23, 2009, telephonic hearing before the circuit court. The parties will also have an opportunity to address the effect, if any, of the Supreme Court’s decision in Padilla v. Kentucky, ___ U.S. ___ (2010), on this case.
 
ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for entry of a new decision.
 
Edward R. Grant

DHS Brief at 3 n.1.

U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RICARDO PEREZ-MORALES
File: AXXX XX5 768 - Eloy, AZ
May 19, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Murray Hilts, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Alec J. Niziolek
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; waiver under former section 212(c) of the Act
 
*1 The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s February 18, 2010, decision denying his motion to reopen proceedings to apply for relief pursuant to former section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act (Act), 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c), in light of the Supreme Court’s decision in INS v. St. Cyr, 533 U.S. 289 (2001). The Department of Homeland Security seeks a summary affirmance. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008); Matter of V-K-, 24 I&N Dec. 500 (BIA 2008).
 
We adopt and affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872 (BIA 1994). Although the respondent concedes that his motion to reopen is untimely, he claims that the filing deadline should be equitably tolled based upon ineffective assistance of counsel (Respondent’s Appeal Brief at 18). The respondent, however, has not complied with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), and the record does not show that his ineffective assistance claim is legitimate. See Ray v. Gonzales, 439 F.3d 582, 588 (9th Cir. 2006).
 
The respondent also contends on appeal that he is eligible for equitable tolling because the Immigration Judge who presided over his 1998 hearing violated due process by failing to advise the respondent of his eligibility for a 212(c) waiver. He also asserts that his appeal waiver was not knowing and voluntary, and a change in the law has resulted in more than a reasonable likelihood that the respondent is eligible for 212(c) relief.
 
We disagree with the respondent’s contentions. See Socop-Gonzalez v. INS, 272 F.3d 1176 (9th Cir. 2001); Valeriano v. Gonzales, 474 F.3d 669, 673 (9th Cir. 2007) (alien was not entitled to equitable tolling of limitations period for filing motion to reopen removal proceedings). The Immigration Judge in 1998 applied the law then applicable, which did not extend section 212(c) relief to the respondent. At the time of the hearing, section 212(c) had been repealed by the Illegal Immigration Reform and Immigrant Responsibility Act of 1996, and the Supreme Court had not yet issued INS v. St. Cyr, supra. Accordingly, the respondent’s reliance upon United States v. Lopez-Velasquez, 568 F.3d 1139 (9th Cir. 2009), is misplaced and given the respondent’s circumstances and the state of the law in 1998, we do not agree with the respondent’s assertion that the Immigration Judge should have advised the respondent that he might be eligible for section 212(c) relief. Valencia v. Mukasey, 548 F.3d 1261 (9th Cir. 2008) (alien lacking apparent eligibility for relief from removal did not have due process right to notice of such relief).
 
*2 The record also does not support the respondent’s claim that his appeal waiver was not knowing, intelligent, and voluntary. The record contains a bi-lingual “Request for Prompt Removal,” signed by the respondent. The respondent agreed that he would waive appeal of the decision ordering his removal, he would accept the order of removal as final, and that he entered the request “voluntarily, knowingly, and intelligently.”
 
The respondent has not established that he exercised due diligence in discovering essential information bearing on his claim. The record reflects that although the respondent’s wife contacted attorneys in 1998, 2008, and 2009, there is no evidence to indicate that the respondent or his wife took any actions to resolve the respondent’s immigration status between 1999 and 2007 (see Declarations of Ricardo Perez-Morales and Christina Perez in Support of Defendant’s Motion to Dismiss Indictment). Valeriano v. Gonzales, supra, at 673 (for equitable tolling to apply, it is necessary that despite all due diligence, the party invoking equitable tolling is unable to obtain vital information bearing on the existence of the claim, and the party’s ignorance of the necessary information must have been caused by circumstances beyond the party’s control).
 
The respondent did not file his motion to reopen until November 2009, which is 8 years after the publication of INS v. St. Cyr, 4 years after the statutory deadline for filing a special motion pursuant to 8 C.F.R § 1003.44(h), and 2 years after the publication of Reynoso-Cisneros v. Gonzales, 491 F.3d 1001, 1002 (9th Cir. 2007) (Respondent’s Appeal Brief at 19-20). See also Lin v. Gonzales, 473 F.3d 979 (9th Cir.2007) (regulation did not preclude jurisdiction over motion to reopen filed by alien who had been lawfully removed after the completion of immigration proceedings).
 
In addition, the record does not contain objective and reliable evidence to establish that the respondent pled guilty in 1994 to possession of cocaine base for sale, nor has the respondent demonstrated that he relied on section 212(c) relief at the time of his 1994 conviction. Thus, there is no basis to disturb the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent is not eligible for a 212(c) waiver or any other relief, and the respondent has failed to show a due process violation.
 
The record reflects that the respondent illegally reentered the United States on multiple occasions. Therefore, he is ineligible for reopening pursuant to 8 C.F.R. 1003.44(k)(2). Avila-Sanchez v. Mukasey, 509 F.3d 1037 (9th Cir. 2007) (provisions of 8 C.F.R. § 1003.44(k)(2) are constitutional). Consistent with the Supreme Court’s decision in INS v. St. Cyr, supra, and the section 212(c) regulation found at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.44(a), the respondent is precluded from establishing his eligibility for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(c) of the Act. Given that the respondent has not established prima facie eligibility for the relief sought, reopening is not warranted. Matter of Gutierrez, 21 I&N Dec. 479, 482 (BIA 1996). In view of the foregoing, the following order shall be entered.
 
*3 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 2224519 (BIA)
End of Document

IN RE: ENRIQUE REYES-FRANCISCO

May 13, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Howard L. Baker, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 The respondent’s timely motion requesting that we reconsider our November 4, 2009, decision denying his motion to reopen will be denied. We rejected the respondent’s ineffective assistance of counsel claim because it was not asserted against a counsel who represented him in his removal proceedings. The respondent asserts error, arguing that the ineffective assistance of counsel claim was properly made against Mario De Marco as the sole principal of the law firm retained by the respondent. However, we recognize as representatives only those attorneys who have properly submitted a notice of appearance and have personally “practiced” before the Executive Office for Immigration Review as that term is defined in 8 C.F.R. § 1001.1 (i). See 8 C.F.R. § § 1001.1(f), (i), (k), (m); 1240.3, 1292.1, 1292.3, 1292.4. An entry of appearance by an attorney who is associated with a law firm does not equate to representation by the firm at large or its principal(s). The record does not contain a notice of entry of appearance from Mr. De Marco. Further, the motion we denied on November 4, 2009, did not establish that Mr. De Marco either appeared at a hearing or filed or prepared a brief or other document on his behalf. We therefore have no basis to assess an ineffective assistance of counsel claim against Mr. De Marco.¿FN1º 8 C.F.R.§ 1001.1(i). Accordingly, the motion to reconsider is denied.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent did not specifically challenge our rejection of his ineffective assistance of counsel claim against Ms. Paige.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice

IN RE: ISSA ELIAS HANNA MURCUS
File: AXXX XX7 189 - Hartford, CT
May 12, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Rashmi N. Patel, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Israel, moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c) to reopen proceedings in which we issued our final order on August 13, 2009. We dismissed the appeal of the February 12, 2008, Immigration Judge order denying a continuance and voluntary departure. The motion requests reopening to apply for adjustment of status. However, as noted during the Immigration Judge hearing and in our decision on appeal, the petition filed by the respondent’s spouse was denied by the United States Citizenship and Immigration Services (USCIS). In the instant motion, the respondent now argues that the attorney who represented him during the hearing, Sharyn D’Urso, provided ineffective assistance in not filing an appeal with the USCIS. However, the motion does not contain the information that might have warranted an appeal with the USCIS. No evidence of the bona fides of the respondent’s marriage has been provided aside from a self serving affidavit from the respondent (Motion at Tab A).
 
Furthermore, insofar as the motion argues that his prior attorney provided ineffective assistance, the motion also fails to comply with the procedural requirements to establish such a claim. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988); see also Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009), vacating 24 I&N Dec. 710 (A.G. 2009). There is a letter to the prior attorney, but no complaint has been filed with the bar authority (see Motion at Tab B). The only explanation for failing to file the bar complaint is that the respondent feared there would be a negative impact on the immigration case. But as the respondent is now represented by current counsel, who also represented him during the immigration appeal process, this explanation is dubious. The respondent has not established that he suffered prejudice as the result of any ineffective assistance of counsel. See Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124 (2nd Cir. 2000). The respondent has not established prima facie eligibility for relief warranting reopening. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The request for a stay is denied as moot.
 
Frederick D. Hess

2010 WL 2224571 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CORINA DE JESUS AREVALO DE JIMENEZ
File: AXXX XX6 703 - Portland, OR
May 11, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Nicole H. Nelson, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Robert F. Peck
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of El Salvador, has appealed the Immigration Judge’s April 29, 2008, decision denying her motion to reopen. Under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3), the Board defers to the factual findings of an Immigration Judge, unless they are clearly erroneous, but it retains independent judgment and discretion, subject to applicable governing standards, regarding pure questions of law and the application of a particular standard of law to those facts. Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
The respondent’s motion to reopen sought reconsideration of her application for Temporary Protected Status (TPS) based upon newly proffered evidence. In order to succeed on a motion to reopen, “the applicant must show that the ¿ profferedº evidence could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing.” Bhasin v. Gonzales, 423 F.3d 977, 984 (9th Cir. 2005); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). The respondent has not made such a showing.
 
The respondent maintains, however, that ineffective assistance of prior counsel is to blame for her failure to submit such evidence during her former hearing. In support of her claim, she submitted an affidavit in which prior counsel admits that he had not properly prepared the respondent’s TPS application. Even if the respondent is deemed to have satisfied the procedural requirements for claims of ineffective assistance of counsel, see Lo v. Ashcroft, 341 F.3d 934, 937-39 (9th Cir. 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), the respondent’s motion was properly denied. We affirm the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that the newly proffered evidence would not alter the determination that the respondent had failed to establish her own — as opposed to her family members’ — continuous residence and physical presence in the United States as of, respectively, February 13, 2001, and March 9, 2001. See Matter of Barrientos, 24 I&N Dec. 100 n. 1 (BIA 2007).
 
Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 2224571 (BIA)

IN RE: JESUS CARDENAS NEGRETE A.K.A. JESUS NEGRETE CARDENAS
File: AXXX XX6 925 - El Centro, CA
May 11, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
*1 This case was last before us on August 25, 2009, at which time we denied the respondent’s motion to reopen and reconsider our previous January 16, 2009, dismissal of the Immigration Judge’s decision. The respondent has now filed a motion on April 9, 2010, which is both untimely and barred by numerical limitations. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion, which will be denied.
 
Because the respondent offers no evidence or argument which he was prevented from presenting at his hearing, we find no independent evidence to support his brief mention of a due process violation or an ineffective assistance of counsel claim.¿FN1º See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 897 (9th Cir. 2003); Ortiz v. INS, 179 F.3d 1148, 1153 (9th Cir. 1999) (finding that prejudice must be shown for a due process challenge and that prejudice is found when the performance of counsel was so inadequate that it may have affected the outcome). We note, particularly, that the respondent appeared pro se before the Immigration Judge and specifically stated that he would represent himself (Tr. at 13-14). The respondent also appeared pro se before the Board during both his appeal and his prior motion. The respondent has not identified an attorney which he claims misrepresented him, and he does not detail any prejudice therefrom. Accordingy, the motion will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Frederick D. Hess

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MICHAEL ALOYSIUS WATSON
File: AXXX XX2 467 - New York, NY
May 11, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Matthew Bray, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Wendy Leifer
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(II) º - Controlled substance violation
  
APPLICATION: Waiver of inadmissibility
 
The respondent appeals from an Immigration Judge’s July 14, 2008, decision denying his application for a waiver of inadmissibility under former section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c). The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) opposes the appeal. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Jamaica and a lawful permanent resident of the United States, has three drug convictions in New York and one drug conviction in New Jersey. The respondent concedes that he is removable under section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(II), but he seeks relief from removal in the form of a waiver of inadmissibility under former section 212(c) of the Act.
 
Former section 212(c) was repealed on April 1, 1997, long before these proceeding commenced, but the Supreme Court has held that such relief remains available to waive grounds of inadmissibility that are based entirely on criminal convictions arising from plea agreements made before that repeal date. INS v. St. Cyr, 533 U.S. 289 (2001). All three of the respondent’s New York convictions resulted from plea agreements made before April 1, 1997, and therefore those convictions can be waived as grounds of inadmissibility under former section 212(c). However, the respondent’s New Jersey conviction presents a more difficult question.
 
On September 28, 1979, a jury in the Superior Court for Essex County, New Jersey, entered a verdict of guilty against the respondent with respect to two crimes: (1) possessing a controlled dangerous substance (marijuana); and (2) possessing a controlled dangerous substance (marijuana) with intent to distribute. After these removal proceedings commenced in 2005, however, the respondent filed a petition for post-conviction relief with the Essex County Superior Court. In response to that petition, the Superior Court issued a June 16, 2006, order vacating the respondent’s 1979 conviction based on ineffective assistance of counsel and then issuing an amended judgment finding him guilty of the same two offenses based on pleas of guilty (rather than a jury verdict). The DHS then amended the charge of removability to reflect this revised conviction date. Under the circumstances, we conclude that the respondent has not demonstrated that his New Jersey drug conviction can be waived as a ground of inadmissibility under former section 212(c).
 
*2 Assuming arguendo that the respondent’s 2006 amended conviction is effective for immigration purposes, we conclude that it cannot be waived as a ground of inadmissibility because the respondent has not demonstrated that it resulted from a plea agreement made before April 1, 1997. See 8 C.F.R. § 1212.3(h)(3) (“Section 212(c) relief is not available with respect to convictions arising from plea agreements made on or after April 1, 1997.”); see also Executive Office for Immigration Review; Section 212(c) Relief for Aliens With Certain Criminal Convictions Before April 1, 1997, 69 Fed. Reg. 57826-01, 57830 (September 28, 2004) (“The alien seeking section 212(c) relief has the burden of … establishing the date on which the alien entered into a plea agreement with the prosecution that resulted in the conviction from which section 212(c) relief is sought.”) Despite the respondent’s argument to the contrary, nothing in the Essex County Superior Court’s 2006 amended judgment specifies that his post hoc plea agreement was made before April 1, 1997. The respondent’s creative assertion that he should be deemed to have pled guilty in 1979 requires a suspension of disbelief that we are simply unwilling to indulge. The Superior Court’s 2006 order clearly states that the 1979 conviction was vacated, and then replaced with a new, plea-based conviction for the same crimes.
 
In the alternative, if we assume that the respondent’s original 1979 conviction remains effective, we would still conclude that it cannot be waived as a ground of inadmissibility. The United States Court of Appeals for the Second Circuit, in whose jurisdiction this proceeding arises, has extended the retroactivity rationale of the Supreme Court’s decision in St. Cyr to certain aliens whose convictions arose from jury trials. See Restrepo v. McElroy, 369 F.3d 627, 634-35 (2d Cir. 2004). However, the court has made clear that section 212(c) relief is available to an alien convicted after a jury trial only if the alien makes an “individualized showing” that section 212(c)’s repeal upset his reasonable, reliance-based interest in the continued availability of that relief. Walcott v. Chertoff, 517 F.3d 149, 155 (2d Cir. 2008); Wilson v. Gonzales, 471 F.3d 111, 122 (2d Cir. 2006). The Immigration Judge found that the respondent had made no such individualized showing (I.J. Dec. at 5-6), and we agree. And while the respondent argues on appeal that the record should be remanded to give him another opportunity to make such an individualized showing, we conclude that he has already had an adequate opportunity to do so.
 
In conclusion, the respondent is removable as charged and has failed to demonstrate that his New Jersey drug conviction can be waived as a ground of inadmissibility under former section 212(c) of the Act. The following order shall be issued.
 
*3 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed and the Immigration Judge’s order of removal is affirmed.
 
Lauri S. Filppu
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 2224569 (BIA)
End of Document

IN RE: RONY SALVADOR CABRERA
File: AXXX XX3 633 - San Diego, CA
May 6, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Matthew Ichinose, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jonathan Grant
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(ii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(ii)º - Convicted of two or more crimes involving moral turpitude
 
Lodged: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony as defined in section 101(a)(43)(G), I&N Act ¿ 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(G)º
  
APPLICATION: Waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(h)
 
This case was last before us on September 11, 2008, when we granted the Department of Homeland Security’s motion to reopen and remand for further proceedings in light of intervening precedent issued by the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, the circuit in which this case arises. After holding additional hearings, the Immigration Judge found that the respondent’s conviction for petty theft with priors under California Penal Code §§ 484(a) and 666 qualified as an aggravated felony under section 101(a)(43)(G) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(G), and rendered the respondent removable under section 237(a)(2)(A)(iii) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a))(2)(A)(iii). The Immigration Judge also found the respondent ineligible for any form of relief from removal.
 
The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision. And the DHS has moved for summary affirmance. In addition, the respondent has filed a motion to remand for further proceedings in light of the April 16, 2010, vacatur and re-sentencing for his petty theft conviction. The respondent’s motion to remand will be granted. The DHS’ motion for summary affirmance is denied. And the respondent’s request for oral argument is denied. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(7).
  
I. ARGUMENTS ON APPEAL AND MOTION
 
On appeal, the respondent has not challenged the Immigration Judge’s finding that he is removable as an aggravated felon. The respondent instead contends that the Immigration Judge erred in finding him ineligible for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(h) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(h), and in denying him the opportunity to present his evidence in support of this application for relief. The respondent argues that section 212(h) of the Act only precludes those who were admitted to the United States as lawful permanent residents and who were convicted of aggravated felonies from obtaining relief. The respondent maintains that section 212(h) does not preclude those, like him, who simply adjusted their status to that of lawful permanent residents from obtaining relief.¿FN1º The respondent bases his argument on Martinez v. Mukasey, 519 F.3d 532 (5th Cir. 2008).
 
*2 In his motion to remand, the respondent contends that his case should be remanded for further proceedings on the issues of his removability and his eligibility for relief. The respondent argues that the Los Angeles Superior Court vacated his 2006 petty offense conviction, the conviction that provided the basis for the Immigration Judge’s finding of removability, and re-sentenced him to 180 days in custody. Accordingly, the respondent maintains that he is no longer removable as an aggravated felon. And the respondent submitted a minute order and other documents in support of this assertion. See Exhibit I to Respondent’s Brief in Support of Motion to Remand.
  
II. MOTION TO REMAND
 
The respondent’s motion to remand is in the nature of a motion to reopen and therefore the respondent must satisfy the requirements set forth in 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c) before he may obtain a remand. See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 471 (BIA 1992). The respondent has met this burden here in that he has offered new and previously unavailable evidence to suggest that he no longer stands convicted of an offense that qualifies as an aggravated felony. See Exhibit I to Respondent’s Brief in Support of Motion to Remand; see also section 101(a)(43)(G) of the Act; Matter of Adamiak, 23 I&N Dec. 878 (BIA 2006) (finding that a conviction vacated for failure of the trial court to advise the alien of the possible immigration consequences of a guilty plea is no longer a valid conviction for immigration purposes).¿FN2º Accordingly, we grant the respondent’s motion to remand, and we remand the record to the Immigration Judge for further consideration of the respondent’s removability and his eligibility for relief from removal in light of his new evidence. Moreover, because we have granted the respondent’s motion to remand, we find it unnecessary to address, at this time, the arguments he has raised on appeal.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to remand is granted and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this order and for the entry of a new decision.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

IN RE: ALEXANDER MOSKALEV
File: AXXX XX7 318 - Detroit, MI
May 5, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
David K. Wenger, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jason A. Ritter
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent appeals the Immigration Judge’s November 4, 2009, decision denying his motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has filed an opposition. The appeal will be sustained, proceedings will be reopened, and the record will be remanded.
 
The relevant procedural history is as follows. The respondent sought to have his removal proceedings continued during the pendency of his wife’s appeal of the denial of her visa petition, of which the respondent is the beneficiary and pursuant to which he could ultimately seek adjustment of status. The Immigration Judge denied his request for a continuance, and the respondent sought and was granted pre-hearing voluntary departure.
 
During the voluntary departure period, the respondent filed a motion to reopen and submitted evidence to support his assertion that his wife’s appeal is likely to be successful, thus warranting a continuance of his proceedings. The Immigration Judge denied the motion to reopen.
 
Thereafter, the respondent retained new counsel and filed a second motion to reopen, the denial of which is the subject of this appeal. This second motion to reopen is based on the allegedly ineffective assistance of prior counsel. The respondent argues that his prior attorneys were deficient in failing to request an interpreter during the proceedings and failing to present available evidence of the bona fides of his marriage.
 
The Board reviews findings of fact, including credibility determinations, under a clearly erroneous standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). We review questions of law, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii).
 
The respondent’s prior attorneys concede that they never asked for an interpreter and never discussed with the respondent the details of voluntary departure before requesting such relief. These actions constitute per se ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent was prejudiced by counsels’ deficient performance because he has shown at least a reasonable possibility of success on the appeal of the denial of the visa petition.
 
Although the respondent’s second motion to reopen is number-barred, the denial of due process arising from the lack of an interpreter and his counsels’ failure to inform him of the nature and consequences of the relief being pursued warrant exercise of our and the Immigration Judge’s authority to sua sponte reopen proceedings. Accordingly, we will sustain the appeal, order the proceedings to be reopened, and remand the record for further proceedings.
 
*2 ORDER: The appeal is sustained, and the Immigration Judge’s November 4, August 18, and July 10, 2009, decisions are vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: These removal proceedings shall be reopened, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD

IN RE: ZARQA NAEEM KHAN
File: AXXX XX6 362 - Baltimore, MD
April 27, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jeffrey B. Steinfeld, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Marvin J. Muller III
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Remand
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Pakistan, appeals the Immigration Judge’s denial of a continuance and order of removal, and requests that the record be remanded. She claims that her former attorney was ineffective in that he did not assist her in pursuing an application for cancellation of removal. The appeal will be sustained, and the record will be remanded for further proceedings.
 
We review the findings of fact made by the Immigration Judge, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i) (2009). We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii); Matter of V-K-, 24 I&N Dec. 500 (BIA 2008); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
The respondent contends that she meets the statutory requirements for cancellation of removal for nonpermanent residents, in that she has been physically present in the United States for a continuous period of over 10 years, has been a person of good moral character during that period, and can establish that her removal would result in exceptional and extremely unusual hardship to her United States citizen mother (Respondent’s Br. at 1). See section 240A(b) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b). She asserts that her prior attorney did not raise the possibility of this form of relief, and when she brought it up, he told her that she had no qualifying relatives and was not eligible. Instead, the attorney pursued a strategy of last resort, requesting continuances until the respondent’s priority date for adjustment of status through her mother became current, or until her husband’s Petition for Alien Worker (Form I-140) was approved (Respondent’s Br. at 8). The Immigration Judge denied the attorney’s last request for a continuance.
 
The respondent has complied with the procedural requirements for claiming ineffective assistance of counsel, set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). See Barry v. Gonzales, 445 F.3d 741 (4th Cir. 2006). Furthermore, we agree with the respondent that she was prejudiced by the actions of her former attorney, Mr. Nielsen. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553, 560 (BIA 2003). In his response to the respondent’s ethics complaint, Mr. Nielsen acknowledges that he did not file an application for cancellation of removal on the respondent’s behalf, and counseled her against it. Even if he genuinely believed that the respondent would not ultimately prevail on such an application, he did not inform her, or the Immigration Court (Tr. at 13), that her mother was a qualifying relative, and he did not adequately discuss with her the strengths and weaknesses of her case. Moreover, Mr. Nielsen admits that he considered the effect of statements she would make in support of her application on her husband’s eligibility for adjustment of status. Thus, his representation of both the respondent and her husband in these circumstances resulted in a conflict of interest that adversely affected the respondent. Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
*2 ORDER: The appeal is sustained.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD

IN RE: RAMIRO A. POLANCO URENA A.K.A. POLANCO RAMIRO A.K.A. EDY RODRIGUEZ

April 26, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Lynn Neugebauer, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Michele Henriques
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: See. 237(a)(2)(A)(ii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(ii)º - Convicted of two or more crimes involving moral turpitude
  
APPLICATION: Relief
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of the Dominican Republic and a lawful permanent resident of the United States since his admission as an immigrant on or about November 8, 1980, has filed a timely appeal from an Immigration Judge’s February 9, 2010, decision. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i) (2010); Matter of R-S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 629 (BIA 2003); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). The Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment and all other issues in an appeal of an Immigration Judge’s decision de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii) (2010), Matter of V-K-, 24 I&N Dec. 500 (BIA 2008); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
Upon our de novo review of the issues presented here, we have concluded that the respondent presents no arguments on appeal that would cause us to disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision. Based on the record before us, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent is subject to removal from the United States based on the respondent’s admissions in this case (Tr. at 10), record of convictions (Group Exh. 2), and concession of removability (Tr. at 10). See section 240(c)(3)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(3)(A). Moreover, the respondent has failed to establish his eligibility for any relief from removal, including cancellation of removal¿FN1º under section 240A(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a). See also section 240(c)(4)(A) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(4)(A); 8 C.F.R. § 1240.8(d).
 
Finally, we consider that accompanying the appeal, the respondent seeks a remand in order to apply for waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(h) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(h). The requirements for a motion to remand are essentially the same as the requirements for a motion to reopen. Rodriguez v. INS, 841 F.2d 865 (9th Cir. 1987). Specifically, “¿aº motion to reopen ¿or remandº proceedings shall not be granted unless it appears to the Board that evidence sought to be offered is material and was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing.” See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). However, the information concerning the health issues affecting his wife and daughter were disclosed by the respondent at the hearing before the Immigration Judge (Tr. at 17). Furthermore, in response to the question posed by the Immigration Judge as to what relief from removal he was seeking, the respondent, through his attorney, stated that “it does not appear that ¿the respondentº is eligible for ¿anyº relief at this point” (Tr. at 11). In addition, the motion must be supported by affidavits, applications for relief and any supporting documentation in order to establish prima facie eligibility for the requested relief upon reopening. Id.
 
*2 Furthermore, to the extent that the respondent may have been prevented from pursuing an application for a section 212(h) waiver by the actions of former counsel, the respondent has not raised the issue of ineffective assistance of former counsel, or complied with the Board’s decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 851 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) (setting out the requirements that would support a motion to reopen based on a claim for ineffective assistance of counsel). Consequently, as the respondent has not demonstrated that a remand is warranted, the motion to remand will be denied.
 
Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
pORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion for a remand is denied.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

In this regard we consider that as the respondent was previously granted a waiver of inadmissibility under former section 212(c) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c), by another Immigration Judge on November 8, 2002, in prior deportation proceedings, the respondent is statutorily precluded by section 240A(c)(6) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(c)(6), from pursuing an application for cancellation of removal for lawful permanent residents.

2010 WL 1747383 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: PABLO JESUS CUADROS-VIVES
File: AXXX XX2 215 - New York, NY
April 20, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
William M. Pavlov, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The final administrative decision on the merits was issued on October 31, 2006. A motion to reopen was previously denied on June 17, 2008. The respondent, a native and citizen of Peru, has reported that a petition for review and a petition for rehearing were denied by the United States Court of Appeals for the Second Circuit. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has not responded to the pending motion to reopen, filed on October 30, 2009. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent seeks reopening of proceedings for consideration of adjustment of status. This motion is both untimely and number barred as it was not filed within 90 days of our October 31, 2006, decision on the merits, and is the second motion to reopen filed by the respondent. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). In the current motion, the respondent initially requested reopening of proceedings for adjustment of status based on a visa petition filed in October 2009 on his behalf by his United States citizen wife, whom he married in August 2009. The visa petition remains pending. The respondent subsequently acknowledged that under Matter of Yauri, 25 I&N Dec. 103 (BIA 2009), jurisdiction of an application for adjustment of status does not lie with the Board or the Immigration Court. We do not find any basis for reopening for termination or administrative closure to pursue adjustment of status under our sua sponte authority. Id.
 
In the June 17, 2008, decision, the Board previously addressed the respondent’s contentions that he was precluded from timely seeking reopening due to ineffective assistance of former counsel and concluded that he had not met his burden of proving that his former attorney, William A. Fernandez, Esquire, was negligent. We separately found that the motion would have been untimely even had we found it appropriate to equitably toll the period for reopening until he employed another attorney. The respondent did not file a motion for reconsideration of that decision with the Board, and as indicated above, the Second Circuit denied a petition for review. We emphasize that the denial of the first motion to reopen was based a finding that the respondent had not shown negligence of his former counsel, as well as on the failure to exercise due diligence in pursuit of the ineffective assistance claim. As we found that ineffective assistance of the first attorney had not been adequately shown on the facts, there can have been no ineffective assistance of the respondent’s subsequent attorney in the failure to diligently pursue the ineffective assistance of counsel claim set forth in the first motion to reopen as the respondent now alleges.
 
*2 The respondent currently seeks reopening to pursue adjustment of status and has conceded that the Board lacks jurisdiction of such an application. Moreover, as the respondent’s marriage to a United States citizen did not occur until 2009, the respondent’s potential eligibility for adjustment of status did not arise until after the period for timely filing a motion to reopen had expired.
 
Accordingly, the motion to reopen, which is untimely and number barred, will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 1747383 (BIA)
