2010 WL 1747423 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARTIN BECERRA-SANCHEZ
File: AXXX XX7 609 - Seattle, WA
April 12, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Devin Theriot-Orr, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Robert F. Peck
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony
 
Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(i)º - Convicted of crime involving moral turpitude
 
Lodged: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled
 
Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I)º - Crime involving moral turpitude
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen his removal proceedings. In our decision dated December 5, 2008, we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision which found him removable as charged above and denied his application for a waiver of inadmissibility under former section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c) ¿1994º. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) opposes the respondent’s motion.¿FN1º The motion will be granted.
  
I. JURISDICTION
 
On February 16, 2000, an Immigration Judge issued an in absentia removal order in the respondent’s case. On May 10, 2000, DHS removed the respondent from the United States. On February 25, 2005, an Immigration Judge granted the respondent’s motion to reopen. We find that the Immigration Judge properly found that he had jurisdiction over the respondent’s motion to rescind the in absentia removal order even though he had been removed from the United States on May 10, 2000. See Matter of Armendarez, 24 I&N Dec. 646, 654 n.6 (BIA 2008) (noting that we were referring to motions to reopen other than those seeking rescission of in absentia removal orders; we expressed no opinion on whether an alien’s departure would foreclose the filing of a rescission motion, as rescission can be viewed as vacating the in absentia removal order from the outset). We thus find that we have jurisdiction over the respondent’s present motion to reopen even though he was removed from the United States in 2000.
  
II.. INEFFECTIVE ASSISTANCE OF COUNSEL CLAIM
 
The respondent’s motion to reopen (filed on December 8, 2009) is untimely. The respondent alleges ineffective assistance of former counsels (Adolfo Ojeda-Casimiro and Amanda Stevens). In Matter of Compean, Bangaly, & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009), vacating 24 I&N Dec. 710 (A.G. 2009), the Attorney General directed the Board to continue to apply the previously established standards for reviewing motions to reopen based on claims of ineffective assistance of counsel (pending the outcome of a rulemaking process).
  
A. The Lozada requirements
 
*2 The respondent shows substantial compliance with the requirements in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), to allege ineffective assistance of Mr. Ojeda-Casimiro and Ms. Stevens. The respondent presents his declaration and the declarations of Mr. Ojeda-Casimiro and Ms. Stevens. We find that the respondent’s failure to file complaints against Mr. Ojeda-Casimiro and Ms. Stevens with appropriate disciplinary authorities is not fatal to his claim under the circumstances presented (as discussed below). See Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 525-26 (9th Cir. 2000) (the failure to comply with the requirements in Matter of Lozada, supra, is not fatal where the alleged ineffective assistance is plain on the face of the administrative record).
  
B. Prejudice
 
The respondent shows prejudice. See Mohammed v. Gonzales, 400 F.3d 785, 793 (9th Cir. 2005) (the alien must generally show that she was prejudiced by her counsel’s performance).
  
1. Aggravated Felony Charge
 
Mr. Ojeda-Casimiro conceded that the respondent (a lawful permanent resident) was convicted of an aggravated felony in the motion to change venue and again at the July 26, 2005, hearing (Tr. at 2-3). Prior to filing a motion to reconsider on January 5, 2009, Ms. Stevens did not advise the respondent to file a motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance of Mr. Ojedo-Casimiro or to timely file a petition for review with the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit. The respondent was convicted of assault in the fourth degree in an Oregon criminal court on November 6, 1995 (Exh. 3). Although not a model of clarity, the conviction record shows “Susp. Imposition¿,º” or imposition of sentence suspended. The conviction record also shows “18 months Formal ¿,º” or 18 months formal probation given. Because the respondent was not sentenced to a term of imprisonment of at least 1 year, the conviction is not an aggravated felony under section 101(a)(43)(F) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(F). The respondent is thus eligible to apply for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a).
  
2. Crime Involving Moral Turpitude Charges
 
Mr. Ojeda-Casimiro also conceded the two crimes involving moral turpitude charges. The respondent shows prejudice on the charge under section 237(a)(2)(A)(i) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(i). In Oregon, assault in the fourth degree is a Class A misdemeanor, and is punishable by a maximum of 1 year in prison. See sections 163.160 (assault in the fourth degree) and 161.615 (prison terms for misdemeanors) of the Oregon Revised Statutes (1995). Because a sentence of 1 year or longer could not be imposed, the conviction is not a deportable offense under section 237(a)(2)(A)(i) of the Act. The respondent also shows prejudice on the charge under section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) of the Act. The conviction fits into the petty offense exception of section 212(a)(2)(A)(ii)(II) of the Act. The maximum penalty possible did not exceed imprisonment for 1 year, and the respondent was not sentenced to any term of imprisonment.
  
C. Due Diligence
 
*3 The respondent shows due diligence. See Ghahremani v. Gonzales, 498 F.3d 993, 999-1000 (9th Cir. 2007) (the limitations period is tolled until the alien definitively learns of counsel’s ineffective assistance). The respondent first learned of both former counsels’ ineffective assistance when he met with present counsel on September 28, 2009. See Motion Exh. A at 3. The motion to reopen was filed on December 8, 2009, within 90 days thereafter. We find equitable tolling of the time limit on the motion to reopen is warranted.
 
We will grant the respondent’s motion to reopen. The respondent shows substantial compliance with the requirements in Matter of Lozada, supra, shows prejudice, and shows due diligence. We will remand to allow the respondent to apply for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Act.¿FN2º Relief from removal is still relevant because the respondent remains removable on the charge under section 212(a)(6)(A)(i) of the Act (present without being admitted or paroled).
 
Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is granted, and the proceedings are reopened.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion.
 
Frederick D. Hess

We deny the respondent’s motion to strike the DHS’s response brief. Pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(g)(3) the Board may, in its discretion, consider a brief filed out of time.

2

An applicant for relief under section 240A(a) of the Act must establish that he or she warrants such relief as a matter of discretion. Matter of C-V-T-, 22 I&N Dec. 7, 10 (BIA 1998).

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GUSTAVO MIRANDA-GONZALEZ
File: AXXX XX8 434 - San Francisco, CA
April 8, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Daniela R. Razawi, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Justin M. Price
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The Board entered the final administrative order in these proceedings on May 25, 2005, when we affirmed a November 17, 2004, decision in which the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion for a continuance, found him removable as an alien convicted of an aggravated felony, and ordered his removal from the United States. On April 2, 2009, the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit denied the respondent’s petition for review of the Board’s decision.¿FN1º See Miranda-Gonzalez v. Holder, 320 Fed. Appx. 807 (9th Cir. 2009). On January 6, 2010, the respondent filed this untimely motion to reopen based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See generally Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). The Department of Homeland Security opposes the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent raises his ineffective assistance of counsel claim against Charles Victor Stebley, Esquire, the attorney who represented him during the removal hearing and on appeal, and who filed the petition for judicial review. The respondent alleges that Attorney Stebley failed to file a written motion with the Immigration Judge to continue the hearing while the respondent pursued a collateral attack on the conviction underlying the charge of removal. See Motion to Reopen at 3.
 
The filing deadline imposed on motions to reopen may be equitably tolled during periods when an alien is prevented from filing because of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Iturribarria v. I.N.S., 321 F.3d 889, 897 (9th Cir. 2003). However, the alien must establish that he suffered prejudice as a result of counsel’s ineffective assistance, and that he exercised due diligence in discovering the ineffective assistance. Id. at 897-98. The respondent has failed to meet this burden.
 
Initially, we do not find that the respondent has shown that he suffered prejudice as a result of Attorney Stebley’s handling of his case. Id. The respondent has not identified any form of relief from removal for which he may qualify. See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 472-73 (BIA 1988) (explaining that a party who seeks a remand or to reopen proceedings to pursue relief bears a “heavy burden” of proving that if proceedings before the Immigration Judge were reopened, with all the attendant delays, the new evidence would likely change the result in the case). Nor has he offered evidence that the conviction underlying the charge of removability has been vacated.¿FN2º As such, the respondent has failed to show that he suffered prejudice as a result of any ineffective assistance of counsel. See Iturribarria v. I.N.S., supra, at 899-900.
 
*2 Secondly, and separately, we are not persuaded that the respondent has adequately shown that he exercised due diligence in this matter. The instant motion to reopen was brought before the Board nearly four years after our May 25, 2005, decision. The respondent states that Attorney Stebley mislead him up to the time that he picked up his file on December 19, 2009, for present counsel to review. However, present counsel entered her notice of appearance on the respondent’s before the Ninth Circuit on May 26, 2006, in conjunction with the petition for review of these very proceedings. The respondent raises no claim regarding present counsel and we do not find any adequate explanation for her apparent failure to raise the issue of ineffective representation with her client years ago. The issue of the Immigration Judge’s denial of the continuance, the adequacy of the evidence of removability, and due process concerns were matters raised before the Ninth Circuit. On this record, we not find that the respondent has exercised due diligence in delaying the filing the present motion with the Board until the denial of his petition for review.
 
Accordingly, the motion to reopen will be denied. The related request for a stay of removal pending the disposition of this motion is denied as moot.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD

1

Citing Grageda v. INS, 12 F.3d 919 (9th Cir. 1993) (finding that the Immigration Judge properly denied a motion to continue because the alien’s conviction was final, and that the pending collateral attack did not affect the conviction’s finality), the Court found that the Immigration Judge did not violate the respondent’s due process rights by refusing to continue his case.

2

The respondent has neither claimed nor offered evidence to show that a collateral attack on the conviction has been filed with the criminal court.

APPLICATION: Reopening; reconsideration
 
*1 The Board entered the final administrative order in these proceedings on September 18, 2008. This case was last before the Board on June 29, 2009, however, when we denied the respondent’s previous motion to reopen and reconsider because it was untimely. On February 26, 2010, the respondent filed the instant motion to reopen and reconsider. The Department of Homeland Security opposes the motion. The motion will be denied.¿FN1º
 
Whether construed as a motion to reopen or as a motion to reconsider, the motion is untimely and number-barred. See sections 240(c)(6)(A), (B) and (7)(A), (C)(ii) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(b)(2), (c)(2). The respondent does not seek to toll the filing restrictions imposed on this motion. See generally Iturribarria v. I.N.S., 321 F.3d 889, 897 (9th Cir. 2003) (recognizing equitable tolling of deadlines and numerical limits on motions to reopen or reconsider during periods when a petitioner is prevented from filing because of ineffective assistance of counsel). Further, it does not appear that any exception to the filing restrictions applies to this case. See generally 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(3).
 
The respondent stresses his equities in the United States as well as the hardship that will accrue to his family as a result of his removal. He has not, however, demonstrated an exceptional situation that would warrant the exercise of our discretionary authority to reconsider a prior Board decision in this case sua sponte or to reopen his proceedings. See Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999) (“As a general matter, we invoke our sua sponte authority sparingly, treating it not as a general remedy for any hardships created by enforcement of the time and number limits in the motions regulations, but as an extraordinary remedy reserved for truly exceptional situations”). Accordingly, the respondent’s motion to reopen and reconsider will be denied.
 
The following order denying the respondent’s untimely, number-barred motion to reopen and reconsider will be entered.¿FN2º
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen and reconsider is denied.

2010 WL 1606961 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: PAMELA VIVIANA MURILLO-VILLALTA
File: AXXX XX3 314 - San Antonio, TX
April 6, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Carla C. Prosper, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jane H. Thomson
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent has filed an untimely and number-barred motion to reopen her removal proceedings, after the Board on July 29, 2003, dismissed her appeal from the Immigration Judge’s denial of her motion to reopen and rescind the in absentia order of removal entered against her on May 3, 2002. The Department of Homeland Security opposes the motion.
 
The respondent has raised an ineffective assistance of counsel claim with regard to her representation below. Upon review of the record, however, we are convinced of an error in our prior decision that warrants sua sponte reconsideration. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). Upon reconsideration, we will sustain the appeal and reopen these removal proceedings based on a determination that proper notice of the scheduled removal hearing was not provided in this case, and the in absentia removal order should therefore be rescinded. Section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C).
 
The basis of the respondent’s motion to reopen below — that notice was not properly provided to her representative of record — was never properly addressed by the Immigration Judge or the Board. Rather, the motion was analyzed primarily as a claim of exceptional circumstances for her failure to appear based on the pending motion for a change of venue, with the Immigration Judge also finding that she had received timely notice through her mother. However, a more careful review of the motion reveals that the respondent claimed in her motion that notice of the scheduled hearing was improper because her attorney had filed a Notice of Appearance as Attorney or Representative Before the Immigration Court (Form EOIR-28) but was not served with any decision after filing the Motion to Change Venue on the respondent’s behalf, and that her attorney was not advised that it had been denied even when he contacted the Immigration Court.
 
Section 239(a)(1) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229(a)(1), provides that written notice of the proceedings shall be provided to the alien “or to the alien’s counsel of record, if any.” Applicable regulations further provide that “¿wºhenever a person is required … to give or be given notice … such notice … shall be given by or to, served by or upon, made by, or requested of the attorney or representative of record, or the person himself if unrepresented.” 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a).
 
In this case, counsel filed a notice of appearance on April 5, 2002, along with a Motion to Change Venue. The record reveals, however, that the Immigration Judge’s April 11, 2002, decision denying the motion was served only on the respondent, not on her counsel of record. In addition, the notice of hearing that was issued on April 11, 2002, was provided only to the respondent, not her attorney of record. Relevant statutes and regulations require that, if an alien is represented, notice must be provided to the alien’s representative of record. That was not done in this case, and the notice was therefore improper.
 
*2 Given the unique circumstances of this case, we therefore find it appropriate to reconsider sua sponte and vacate our prior decision in this case, and to sustain the respondent’s appeal and reopen these proceedings and rescind the in absentia order of removal based on a lack of proper notice. In view of this decision, we need not address the numerous other issues raised in the respondent’s motion to reopen. Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The Board’s July 29, 2003, decision is reconsidered sua sponte and vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is sustained and the Immigration Judge’s June 27, 2002, decision denying the motion to reopen is sustained.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to reopen is granted, the Immigration Judge’s May 3, 2002, in absentia order of removal is vacated, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD

End of Document

2010 WL 1606976 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MOHAMED AHMED HUSSEIN SALEH
File: AXXX XX7 479 - New York, NY
April 1, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ehab Moustafa, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º- In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Egypt, appeals from an Immigration Judge’s decision dated March 4, 2009, denying the respondent’s motion to reopen his removal proceedings that were conducted in absentia on January 24, 2006. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
We affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen his removal proceedings that were conducted in absentia. Specifically, we find no error in the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that the respondent did not demonstrate that the in absentia order of removal qualifies for rescission. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii); see also section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). Nor do we find clear error in the factual findings leading up to that determination. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i). Pursuant to section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act, an order issued following proceedings conducted in absentia may be rescinded only upon a motion to reopen filed (a) within 180 days after the date of the order of removal if the alien demonstrates that he failed to appear because of exceptional circumstances, or (b) at any time if the alien demonstrates he did not receive proper notice of the hearing, or because he was in Federal or State custody and failed to appear through no fault of his own.
 
The respondent on appeal does not assert either that he was unaware of or that he did not receive proper notice of his January 24, 2006, hearing that was scheduled to begin at 9:30 a.m. Instead, the respondent apparently asserts that his failure to timely file a motion alleging exceptional circumstances for his failure to appear is the result of the ineffective assistance of his former attorneys.
 
The respondent’s motion is untimely as it was filed well after 180 days, 3 years, after the date of the order of removal and, in any event, establishes no exceptional circumstances that would excuse the respondent’s failure to appear. Moreover, we reject the respondent’s apparent ineffective assistance of counsel claim. Significantly, the respondent on appeal did not submit an affidavit averring to his attorney’s assertion that the respondent’s former attorneys caused or contributed to the delay in filing a motion to reopen. See, e.g., Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503, 506 (BIA 1980) (recognizing that statements made by counsel are not evidence). Notably, the respondent’s brief on appeal indicates that the first attorney informed the respondent of the need to file a motion to reopen within a certain time period (Respondent’s brief at 1). In addition, the respondent has not indicated when, after learning about the need to file a motion to reopen, he first contacted the second attorney (although he apparently did not actually hire that attorney until February 2008).
 
*2 We do not find that the respondent’s current attorney’s assertion relating to the ineffectiveness of the respondent’s former attorneys is alone sufficient to demonstrate patent error. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Nor, has the respondent sufficiently complied with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra. See also Matter of Compean, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009) (directing Board to apply Lozada standards to ineffective assistance of counsel claims pending the outcome of a rulemaking process). Therefore, on this record, and on the facts as found by the Immigration Judge, we decline to set aside the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen.
 
Finally, even if the regulatory requirements set forth at 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c) and 1003.23(b)(3) were applicable, the respondent’s motion is untimely, as it was filed well over 90 days, 3 years, after the Immigration Judge’s 2006 order of removal. The respondent has not identified any changed circumstances in Egypt that would have excepted his untimely motion from the applicable time limitations. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(3)(ii). Moreover, the “new” evidence relating to the respondent’s approved visa petition does not pertain to any claim of a material change in country conditions in his native Egypt since the Immigration Judge’s decision. Thus, the respondent has failed to show that his motion to reopen falls within an exception to the general time limits. See section 240(c)(7)(C) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(3)(ii). As a result, the respondent is precluded from seeking reopening to apply for adjustment of status.
 
On the whole, the record supports the Immigration Judge’s resolution. Accordingly, we will enter the following order.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Linda S. Wendtland
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 1606976 (BIA)

2010 WL 1606977 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: KATARZYNA MARTA PAWLIK
File: AXXX XX2 881 - Chicago, IL
April 1, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
David Butbul, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
David M. Williams
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º- In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Poland, appeals an Immigration Judge’s March 13, 2008, decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen. We review the Immigration Judge’s factual findings for clear error and all other issues de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3). The appeal will be dismissed.
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge erred by denying the respondent’s motion to reopen because the respondent was not aware that she was requesting voluntary departure at her December 18, 2007, hearing. See Respondent’s Brief at 1-2. We disagree.
 
Pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1), to obtain reopening, the respondent must show that the evidence she seeks to admit is material, previously unavailable and could not have been discovered or presented at her previous hearing. The respondent has not met this burden. In her motion to reopen, the respondent argued that she believed that, because she received a fingerprint notice dated April 26, 2007, she would be allowed to remain in the United States. See Motion to Reopen at 1-2. Despite this argument, however, the notice was issued eight months prior to her hearing on December 18, 2007, at which the respondent requested voluntary departure. See Attachment to Motion to Reopen. Also, at her hearing, the respondent’s then-attorney clearly requested voluntary departure. Although the record is unclear as to whether the Immigration Judge was accurate in indicating that the respondent’s former counsel consulted with her about the voluntary departure request during the hearing (I.J. at 2; Tr. at 9-12), the fact remains that the respondent has not contended that her former counsel rendered ineffective assistance; nor has she complied with the legal requirements for such a contention. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988); see also Matter of Compean, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009) (directing the Board to apply the Lozada standards to ineffective assistance of counsel claims pending the outcome of a rulemaking process).
 
Moreover, the respondent’s former attorney was previously given a seven-month continuance to prepare the respondent’s case (I.J. at 1; Tr. at 1-2). Despite this continuance, the only relief that the respondent’s former attorney requested at her subsequent hearing was a period of voluntary departure “to the maximum extent the Court can grant her” (Tr. at 9). The Immigration Judge did require the respondent to waive all appeal rights as a condition to receiving the maximum 120-day period for voluntary departure, but such a requirement exists as a matter of law. See Matter of Ocampo-Ugalde, 22 I&N Dec. 1301 (BIA 2000). Further, although the respondent’s former counsel initially contested the legal validity of the Notice to Appear, he (and thus the respondent) ultimately abandoned his challenges when — after the Immigration Judge accepted an amendment to the Notice to Appear but otherwise ruled against the legal challenges — counsel conceded removability and waived appeal in order to receive the maximum voluntary departure period. Given these facts, we find no error in the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen.
 
*2 Finally, we note that all motions to reopen based on an application for relief “must be accompanied by the appropriate application.” See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). Here, despite requesting reopening, the respondent does not allege that she is eligible for any relief from removal. See Respondent’s Brief at 2; Notice of Appeal. Thus, the respondent has not demonstrated that she suffered prejudice from the Immigration Judge’s alleged error. See Eke v. Mukasey, 512 F.3d 372, 383 (7th Cir. 2008). Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Linda S. Wendtland
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 1606977 (BIA)
End of Document

2010 WL 1606999 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ALLAN GUILLERMO RAMOS-GARCIA
File: AXXX XX9 122 - Oakdale, LA
March 31, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Daniel Redwood
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony
  
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; reopening
 
On January 28, 2010, the respondent filed a timely motion to reconsider the Board’s January 8, 2010, decision finding him removable as charged. He filed a second motion to reconsider on February 4, 2010, and filed a motion to reopen on March 8, 2010. The respondent also submitted an opposition to one of the Department of Homeland Security’s (“DHS”) submissions which we construe as a second motion to reopen, filed March 19, 2010. The record will be remanded.
 
In his various motions the respondent argues that his conviction for indecent behavior with a juvenile in violation of Louisiana Revised Statutes § 14:81 was not an aggravated felony. He argues that the statute is divisible, and it is unclear under what section of the statute he was convicted. As such, he maintains that the record cannot support a finding that he was convicted of an aggravated felony. The respondent also essentially contends that his former attorney provided him with ineffective assistance by erroneously advising him that his noted conviction constituted an aggravated felony, and in advising him to concede removability. In addition, he avers that he is not subject to removal as he is a national of the United States.
 
We initially find no merit to the respondent’s argument that he is a national of the United States because he served in the United States military, at which time he pledged an oath of allegiance to the United States (3/8/10 Motion at 1). Contrary to the respondent’s arguments otherwise, service in the armed forces of the United States and the taking of the standard military oath does not make a person a national of this country. See Warmington v. Keisler, 254 Fed. Appx. 287, 289 (5th Cir. 2007), citing Reyes-Alcaraz v. Ashcroft, 363 F.3d 937, 938-40 (9th Cir. 2004) (holding that service in the armed forces of the United States, along with the taking of the standard military oath, does not alter an alien’s status to that of a “national” within the meaning of the Act); see also Omolo v. Gonzales, 452 F.3d 404, 409 (5th Cir. 2006) (finding that “a person may become a national only by birth or by completing the naturalization process”).
 
With the respondent’s February 4, 2010, motion he submitted evidence that he sent a letter to former counsel advising her of his intent to pursue a claim of ineffective assistance against her.¿FN1º In his March 19, 2010, response to a DHS submission, the respondent reported that he had complied with the requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, appending a copy of an affidavit from himself, and a bar complaint he reportedly filed against former counsel. As with another submission, these submissions and associated arguments constitute a motion to reopen. Id.
 
*2 This Board is cognizant of the number limitations on motions to reconsider and reopen. Therefore, to resolve any ambiguity in this regard, we shall accept the entirety of the respondent’s submissions sua sponte. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). We find that the respondent has substantially satisfied the requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel as enumerated in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); see also Lara v. Trominski, 216 F.3d 487 (5th Cir. 2000). As a result of counsel’s concession to the charge of removability, the respondent was precluded from presenting any argument in this regard. Such action served to prejudice the respondent. See De Zavala v. Ashcroft, 385 F.3d 879, 884 (5th Cir. 2004) (requiring a showing of prejudice for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel).
 
In light of the record before us, and affording the respondent every benefit of the doubt, we find it appropriate to remand the record to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings. On remand, the respondent should be allowed an opportunity to contest the charge of removability. Therefore, the following orders shall be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings in accordance with this decision.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

This aspect of the respondent’s motion, therefore, is one seeking reopening as he has introduced new evidence which he wishes the Board to consider. Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c).

KeyCite Red Flag - Severe Negative Treatment
    1  Review Granted, Judgment Vacated by Umer v. Holder, 5th Cir., March 6, 2012
2010 WL 1606998 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: KHALID UMER
File: AXXX XX2 967 - San Antonio, TX
March 31, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Vinh C. Trieu, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Carmen A. Leal
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony
 
Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(ii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(ii)º - Convicted of two or more crimes involving moral turpitude
  
APPLICATION: Remand; 212(c) waiver
 
This case was last before us on March 26, 2007, when we granted the respondent’s unopposed motion to reopen, and remanded the record to the Immigration Court for further proceedings. On June 3, 2008, the Immigration Judge issued a new decision denying the respondent’s application for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c) (repealed 1996), and ordering the respondent removed to Pakistan. The respondent has now appealed this decision. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has requested that the Immigration Judge’s decision be affirmed. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility, made by the Immigration Judge under a “clearly erroneous” standard. We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met their relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(d)(3)(i), (ii); see also Matter of V-K-, 24 I&N Dec. 500 (BIA 2008); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
The respondent first argues on appeal that the Immigration Judge erred in refusing new counsel’s request to amend the pleadings in remanded proceedings after the Board granted the respondent’s motion to reopen his case based on ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent further argues that his theft offense conviction under section 31.03 of the Texas Penal Code was divisible, and that the DHS did not meet its burden of establishing that the respondent was convicted of the portion of the statute which qualified his offense as an aggravated felony.
 
We initially note that the respondent on appeal has mischaracterized the Board’s March 26, 2007, decision granting his unopposed motion to reopen. Contrary to the respondent’s assertions, the Board made no explicit or implicit “determination that previous counsel was ineffective,” and further, that it should then be “assume¿dº that any pleading taken during previous proceedings was without the advice of effective counsel at that time” (see Respondent’s Br. at 3, 9). There was nothing in the Board’s March 26, 2007, decision which required the Immigration Judge to allow him to change his pleadings simply because new counsel would “like to deny some of the allegations” (Tr. at 3-11). See, e.g., Hoodho v. Holder, 558 F.3d 184, 187 (2d Cir. 2009) (“A petitioner cannot disavow that concession because, in hindsight, it might have been preferable for him to have contested removability, rather than to have conceded it.”).
 
*2 The respondent did not clearly articulate an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as it related to his pleadings in his motion to the Board (his motion alleges only ineffective assistance of counsel in failing to file a 212(c) waiver application) or before the Immigration Judge at his remanded proceedings (Tr. at 3-11; see Respondent’s Motion to Reopen). The respondent’s arguments regarding the alleged error in prior counsel’s concessions he now raises on appeal were not brought before the Immigration Judge, and we further note that the respondent has not filed an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as it relates to counsel’s judicial admissions under Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). The distinct and formal concession of removability, made by the respondent’s attorney acting in his professional capacity and in the respondent’s presence during the removal hearing, is binding on the respondent as a judicial admission. The respondent in the instant case has not established that circumstances exist that would permit the withdrawal of his prior judicial admissions. Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377, 382 (BIA 1986) (absent egregious circumstances, a distinct and formal admission made as a tactical decision by an attorney in a deportation proceeding is binding on his alien client); cf. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(2)(i)(B) (authorizing summary dismissal of appeals when “¿tºhe only reason for the appeal specified by the party concerned involves a finding of fact or a conclusion of law that was conceded by that party at a prior proceeding”); see generally Pool Co. v. Cooper, 274 F.3d 173, 185 (5th Cir. 2001) (finding that the court was entitled to treat a concession as a binding judicial admission of fact.); Martinez v. Bally’s Louisiana, Inc., 244 F.3d 474 (5th Cir 2001) (a judicial admission is conclusive, unless the court allows it to be withdrawn). Accordingly, we will dismiss the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s determination that he is removable as charged on the Notice to Appear (NTA).
 
Moreover, even if we were to consider arguendo the respondent’s contentions on appeal challenging his removability, we find his arguments to be unavailing. The record includes undisputed evidence showing that the respondent was convicted of tampering with government records in the third degree in 1989, and of theft in the third degree in 1992, in violation of Texas law (Gr. Exh. 6A). We note that the respondent on appeal has not challenged his deportability under section 237(a)(2)(A)(ii) of the Act, and he thus remains removable under this charge.
 
With regard to the charge of removability under section 237(a)(2)(A)(iii) of the Act, as an alien convicted of an aggravated felony, the respondent has argued that section 31.03 of the Texas Penal Code, the statute under which he was convicted, is a divisible statute. Specifically, he has argued that the statute does not “necessarily” require “an intent to deprive the owner of his property” (Respondent’s Br. at 8-9). This assertion, however, is contravened by a plain reading of section 31.03 of the Texas Penal Code, which provides, in relevant part, that a person commits a theft offense “if he unlawfully appropriates property with intent to deprive the owner of property.” Section 31.03(a) of the Texas Penal Code (emphasis added). Accordingly, “intent to deprive the owner of property” is an essential element of theft under Texas law. Moreover, even if the respondent was convicted under a divisible statute, his record of conviction establishes that his conviction was an aggravated felony as described in section 101(a)(43)(G) of the Act¿FN1º under the modified categorical approach.
 
*3 The record of conviction shows that the respondent in 1992 entered a plea of nolo contendre to “¿tºheft (sting operation), with intent to deprive the owner,” and the indictment charged the respondent, “with intent to deprive the owner…of the property…¿to wit: 90 carton of cigarettesº and believing said property was stolen by another, appropriate, by acquiring and otherwise exercising control over said property” (Exh. 6A). The respondent was sentenced to 2 years of imprisonment for this crime (id.). In Matter of Cardiel-Guerrero, 25 I&N Dec. 12 (BIA 2009) and Matter of Bahta, 22 I&N Dec. 1381 (BIA 2000), the Board held that the generic and contemporary meaning of “receipt of stolen property” described in section 101(a)(43)(G) of the Act prohibits the concealing, selling, or withholding of stolen or extorted property, or aiding in same, knowing it to have been so stolen or obtained. The Texas theft offense for which the respondent was convicted and sentenced to a term of imprisonment exceeding 1 year, falls within the generic definition of “receipt of stolen property” relied upon by the Board in its precedent decisions, and constitutes an aggravated felony under section 101(a)(43)(G) of the Act. Accordingly, the respondent also remains removable under section 237(a)(2)(A)(iii) of the Act, as an alien convicted of an aggravated felony.
 
Turning to the respondent’s application for relief from removal, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent cannot obtain a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(c) of the Act because there is no comparable ground of inadmissibility to section 101(a)(43(G) of the Act, the category that underpins his aggravated felony charge under section 237(a)(2)(A)(iii) of the Act. See Matter of Brieva-Perez, 23 I&N Dec. 766, 771 (BIA 2005), pet’n for review denied by Brieva v. Gonzales, 482 F.3d 356 (5th Cir. 2007); Matter of Blake, 23 I&N Dec. 722 (BIA 2005); see also 8 C.F.R. 1212.3(f)(5). The test for determining whether a ground of removability has a statutory counterpart in section 212(a) of the Act “turns on whether Congress has employed similar language to describe substantially equivalent categories of offenses.” Matter of Brieva-Perez, supra, at 771, citing Matter of Blake, supra. No provision in section 212(a) of the Act establishes inadmissibility for theft or “receipt of stolen property” or any comparable category of offenses. We recognize that a theft offense is ordinarily a crime involving moral turpitude and thus a conviction therefor will render an alien inadmissible under section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) of the Act. Nonetheless, that fact does not establish comparability under the standard announced in Matter of Brieva-Perez, supra, and Matter of Blake, supra. The wide range of offenses that fall within the scope of section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) of the Act simply does not compare in any way that can be described as substantially equivalent to the narrow category of theft offenses specified in section 101(a)(43)(G) of the Act. Cf. Matter of Brieva, 23 I&N Dec. 766 (BIA 2005) (holding that section 212(a) of the Act does not contain a ground of inadmissibility that corresponds to the crime or violence ground of removability). Thus, we conclude that section 212(a) of the Act does not contain a ground of inadmissibility that is comparable to the grounds upon which the respondent is removable. Accordingly, we affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision to pretermit the respondent’s application for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(c).
 
*4 Finally, the respondent claims that the Immigration Judge erred in “fail¿ingº to consider other forms of relief respondent may assert to avoid removal,” and that he intends to apply for benefits under section 244 of the Act, as the spouse of a temporary protected status beneficiary (Respondent’s Br. at 14). We note, however, that the respondent was asked by the Immigration Judge what relief from removal he wanted to pursue, and he never stated he had any intention to file an application for benefits under section 244 of the Act (Tr. at 11-12, 30). The respondent on appeal has not otherwise submitted an application for relief from removal, or presented new evidence establishing his prima facie eligibility for derivative temporary protected status¿FN2º or any other relief from removal that may have justified remanding his case for further proceedings (Respondent’s Br. at 14). See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992) (a motion to remand may be denied where a prima facie case for the relief sought has not been established, or in the absence of previously unavailable, material evidence).
 
In view of the foregoing, the following order shall be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Garry D. Malphrus
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Section 101(a)(43)(G) of the Act defines as an aggravated felony, “a theft offense (including receipt of stolen property) or burglary offense for which the term of imprisonment at least one year”.

2

See sections 244(c)(1), (2) of the Act (eligibility standards for temporary protected status); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1244.2, 1244.3, 1244.4 (same).

2010 WL 1607006 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GABRIEL FELIX-FERNANDEZ
File: AXXX XX8 975 - San Diego, CA
March 30, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Robin Chandler Carr, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
J.L. Woodmansee
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 We entered the final administrative order in this case on March 29, 2006, and denied a subsequent motion to reopen in 2007. On October 8, 2009, the respondent filed a second motion to reopen arguing that he received ineffective assistance of counsel from the attorney who represented him at his initial hearings before the Immigration Judge. Alternatively, the respondent requests that we reconsider his case in light of the decision by the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit in de Rodriquez -Echeverria v. Mukasey, 534 F.3d 1047 (9th Cir. 2008). The Department of Homeland Security opposes the motion, which will be denied.
 
We are not persuaded by the respondent’s excuse for failing to comply with the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See also Matter of Compean, Bangaly & J-E-C-, 25 I&N Dec. 1 (A.G. 2009), vacating 24 I&N Dec. 710 (A.G. 2009). See also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) (requiring a showing of prejudice in order to prevail on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel). Although prior counsel no longer practiced at the location where his office was previously located, the respondent has not explained what reasonable steps were taken to determine if he was still practicing law, and where.
 
In any event, the respondent has not shown that he exercised due diligence in pursuing a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel against Robert B. Schneider, Esquire. Although it is not specifically referenced in the motion, the respondent’s current counsel, Ms. Carr, represented the respondent at his final hearing before the Immigration Judge in 2005, on appeal to the Board (when she received a full copy of the transcript), and on his first motion to reopen in 2007. If the respondent felt that Mr. Schneider’s strategy and efforts at the initial hearings in the immigration court constituted ineffective assistance, such a claim could have been pursued years ago. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 897 (9th Cir. 2003)(holding that equitable tolling will be applied where the alien is prevented from timely filing a motion by deception, fraud, or error so long as the alien acted with due diligence in discovering the deception, fraud, or error). See also Ghahremani v. Gonzales, 498 F.3d 993 (9th Cir. 2007)(a demonstration of “unbroken efforts to retain competent counsel and file a motion to reopen” is necessary to establish due diligence warranting equitable tolling). Accordingly, the motion to reopen based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel will be denied as untimely and number-barred.
 
*2 The respondent’s alternative argument also fails. De Rodriquez - Echeverria v. Mukasey, supra, does not represent a fundamental change in law. See Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 1999) (emphasizing the importance of administrative finality and finding that the Board will exercise sua sponte authority to reconsider a case under new law only when the change in law is fundamental in nature and not merely an incremental development in the state of the law). In De Rodriquez -Echeverria the court remanded the case to us to address, in the first instance, whether 8 C.F.R. § 287.3(c) was violated and the statements were voluntary in the circumstances of that case. Further, as the DHS points out in its opposition brief, the respondent has not addressed the Ninth Circuit’s subsequent decision in Samayoa-Martinez v. Holder, 558 F.3d 897, 901-02 (9th Cir. 2009), which was issued seven months before this motion was filed. In Samayoa-Martinez the court held that 8 C.F.R.§ 287.3(c) does not require that DHS advise an alien of a right to counsel until the alien is placed in formal proceedings by the filing of the Notice to Appeal in the immigration court. The court also noted that § 287.3(c) does not include any requirement that the alien be advised of a right to remain silent or a right against self-incrimination.¿FN1º Id. at n. 6. Accordingly, the respondent has not demonstrated a fundamental change in law that impacts the result in this case and the motion to reconsider based on De Rodriquez-Echeverria v. Mukasey, supra, will also be denied as untimely.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

We note that the respondent’s Form I-213 (Exh. 4) and the Report of Investigation (Exh. 5) were not admitted into evidence until the May 13, 2005, hearing, at which time the respondent was represented by Ms. Carr, who affirmatively stated that she had no objection to their admission (Tr. at 51).

2010 WL 1607037 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: BONAMY CELESTE BUCHHOLZ
File: AXXX XX8 090 - San Antonio, TX
March 29, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jose M. Guerrero, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jane H. Thomson
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of South Africa, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s August 7, 2008, denial of her motion to reopen her deportation proceedings. During those proceedings, she was granted voluntary departure. The respondent withdrew her request for voluntary departure and requested reopening to pursue her pending application for adjustment of status. The Department of Homeland Security opposes the respondent’s motion to reopen.
 
We review the findings of fact, including the determination of credibility made by the Immigration Judge, under a “clearly erroneous” standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i) (2009). We review all other issues, including whether the parties have met the relevant burden of proof, and issues of discretion, under a de novo standard. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008); Matter of V-K-, 24 I&N Dec. 500 (BIA 2008).
 
In her motion to reopen, the respondent claimed that her prior counsel was ineffective in that he did not pursue the respondent’s pending application for adjustment of status as a stepchild of a United States citizen. The Immigration Judge found that the motion was untimely. He further concluded that the appellant had not complied with the requirements for claiming ineffective assistance of counsel under Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
On appeal, the respondent makes no argument concerning the untimeliness of her motion to reopen, and we find no error in the Immigration Judge’s determination that the motion to reopen was not timely filed by the respondent’s current attorney. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(2)(motions to reopen shall be filed within 90 days of final administrative removal order).
 
We further agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent did not comply with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra. Specifically, she did not submit an affidavit from the respondent setting forth in detail the allegations against her former counsel, and she did not demonstrate that her former attorney had been informed of the allegations and had an opportunity to respond (I.J. at 3). Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639; see also Lara v. Trominski, 216 F.3d 487, 497 (5th Cir. 2000) (upholding the general application of the Lozada rules). The fact that the respondent submitted a copy of the complaint against her former attorney is not sufficient (Respondent’s Br. at 2). First, the respondent submitted no proof of filing with, or receipt by, the State Bar of Texas. Second, the respondent’s statement in the complaint does not serve as an affidavit. Third, the respondent has submitted nothing to demonstrate that her former attorney received a copy of the complaint or any other information concerning her claim of ineffective assistance. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.¿FN1º
 
*2 ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

While we need not address whether the respondent has demonstrated sufficient prejudice in her claim of prior ineffective assistance, we note that the United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit has ruled that the failure to receive relief that is purely discretionary in nature does not amount to a deprivation of a constitutional liberty interest. Assaad v. Ashcroft, 378 F.3d 471, 475-76 (5th Cir. 2004).

2010 WL 1607076 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MAURICIO MORA-DURAN A.K.A. MAURICIO MORA
File: AXXX XX3 602 - Chicago, IL
March 26, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Raul Villasuso, Jr., Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Vanessa G. Pocock
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before the Board on September 17, 2009, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal alleging that the voluntary departure bond set by the Immigration Judge was excessive and found that the respondent’s due process rights had not been violated by the Immigration Judge’s denial of a motion to withdraw as counsel filed by his attorney. The respondent, a male native and citizen of Mexico, has filed a timely motion to reopen for consideration of cancellation of removal. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes the motion, which will be denied.
 
A motion to reopen proceedings shall be supported by affidavits or other evidentiary material and shall not be granted unless it appears that the evidence offered is material and was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). A motion to reopen will not be granted unless the alien establishes a prima facie case of eligibility for the underlying relief sought. See INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94 (1988). The respondent also claims that reopening is warranted because he received ineffective assistance from his former attorney which resulted in the failure to file an application for cancellation of removal below.
 
The respondent acknowledges that he could have filed a cancellation application with supporting evidence for consideration at the 2008 hearing. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). While the respondent has submitted an application for cancellation in support of this motion, he has not provided an affidavit or any other documentary evidence demonstrating prima facie eligibility for cancellation of removal as required for reopening. See INS v. Abudu, supra. We further note that the respondent has complied with none of the requirements of Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003), and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), for making a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Stroe v. INS, 256 F.3d 498, 501-02 (7th Cir. 2001).¿FN1º Under the circumstances, the respondent has not shown that reopening is warranted.
 
Accordingly, the motion to reopen will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
*2 Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent was represented below and on appeal by Malcolm B. Gerber, Esquire. The respondent addressed an unsworn statement, dated October 16, 2009, to Calmec Global Law Group generally stating that he was never given an opportunity to present an application for relief from removal for adjudication. This statement is inadequate to satisfy the Lozada requirement that the respondent notify Mr. Gerber of the charges of ineffective assistance raised and to provide Mr. Gerber with the opportunity to respond. In addition, although the respondent has provided a copy of an document signed by both the respondent and Mr. Gerber, this document is primarily in Spanish and is inadequate to set forth the nature and scope of their agreement, as it is not accompanied by an English translation. The respondent has also completed a copy of a form requesting investigation of the Calmec Global Group rather than Mr. Gerber, and there is no showing that the form has been filed with the Illinois bar.

2010 WL 1607090 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ROLAND SHABAN LLULLA
File: AXXX XX1 344 - Dallas, TX
March 26, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Mark E. Jacobs, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent appeals from the decision of the Immigration Judge dated December 7, 2008, denying his motion to reopen to allow him to apply for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b). The appeal will be sustained.
 
In a decision dated August 5, 2008, the Immigration Judge found that the respondent had abandoned his application for cancellation of removal (Form EOIR 42B) due to his failure to file such application on or before the April 7, 2008, deadline set by the Immigration Judge (Tr. at 74). The respondent was ordered removed to Albania.
 
The respondent timely filed a motion to reopen demonstrating that the application and fee had been received by the U.S. Citizenship and Immigration Service (USCIS) on January 28, 2008, and asserting that he believed his former attorney had filed the application with the Immigration Court. Although he had “fired” his attorney in late January 2008, he asserts that he believed his former attorney had done all that was necessary as far as filing his application with the Immigration Court. The respondent states that he was unaware of his former counsel’s filing of a motion to withdraw, which alleges that the respondent was informed of his obligation to file the Form EOIR-42B. He states that he only learned in June 2008 that his cancellation application had not been filed with the Immigration Court after he received his file from his former attorney, and he points out that he requested additional time to file said application in a motion filed with the Immigration Court on August 1, 2008.
 
The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposed reopening, and the Immigration Judge determined that reopening was not warranted. The Immigration Judge noted that the respondent had not complied with any of the requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Additionally, the respondent’s notarized verification stating that “on his oath … each and every statement contained ¿in the motion to reopenº is within my personal knowledge and is true and correct” was legally insufficient to prove any of the facts asserted in the motion.
 
The regulations provide that the Immigration Judge may set and extend time limits for the filing of applications and that an application shall be deemed waived if not filed within the time set by the Immigration Judge. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.31(c). In the instant case, the respondent admittedly missed the April 7, 2008, deadline set by the Immigration Judge for filing a Form EOIR-42B with the court.
 
*2 However, the facts set forth in the respondent’s motion, the truth of which he affirmed in a sworn statement, as well as errors contained in the former attorney’s motion to withdraw cast doubt on whether the respondent’s duty to file the EOIR-42B with the court was effectively communicated to him following his attorney’s withdrawal. The respondent’s former attorney indicated in his motion that such motion was delivered to the respondent. However, as pointed out by the respondent, the motion to withdraw identifies as his last known address an address in New Jersey, to which he had indicated in his motion to change venue he intended to move, rather than his address of record in Arlington, Texas.
 
Additionally, the motion lists an incorrect deadline for the filing of the Form EOIR-42B. See Immigration Court Practice Manual § 2.3(i)(ii)(attorney seeking withdrawal must provide evidence that he or she notified or attempted to notify the alien, with a recitation of specific efforts made, of pending deadlines, the necessity of meeting deadlines, and the consequences of failing to meet deadlines). Nor does it appear that a copy of the Immigration Judge’s order granting the motion to withdraw was sent to the respondent. Although the respondent, who attributes the failure to timely file to “miscommunication” rather than former counsel’s “ineffectiveness,” should have at least complied with the Lozada requirement of informing his former counsel of the allegations made against him, the errors contained in the motion to withdraw cast doubt on whether the respondent was informed by former counsel that he was expected to file the application with the court.
 
Additionally, it is apparent that the respondent did not intend to abandon his application, as the application and fee had already been sent to USCIS, and the respondent filed a motion several weeks before the scheduled hearing explaining the failure to timely file the application and requesting additional time to do so. The respondent has been in the United States for 17 years and has close family ties here. Under the circumstances, we find that proceedings should be reopened to give the respondent an opportunity to seek cancellation of removal. Accordingly, the respondent’s appeal will be sustained, and the record will be remanded to the Immigration Court.
 
ORDER: The appeal is sustained.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to reopen is granted, the proceedings are reopened, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and the entry of a new decision.
 
Hugh G. Mullane
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 1607090 (BIA)

*1 The respondent’s motion to reopen, which is opposed by the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS”), will be denied.¿FN1º
 
An Immigration Judge on December 17, 2003, denied the respondent’s motion to terminate and found the respondent subject to removal based on alien smuggling. The Board affirmed this decision without opinion on March 14, 2005. The respondent, who is from Mexico, filed a motion to reopen on January 28, 2010. The motion was filed many years out of time. 2010 WL 1607055 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE ESTEBAN URGILES-LLIVICHUZHCA
File: AXXX XX5 017 - New York, NY
March 18, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Daniel W. Diaz, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Remand
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Ecuador, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated September 25, 2008. Based on admissions of the allegations of fact and concession of the charge of removability made in the Notice to Appear, the Immigration Judge found the respondent removable for being present in the United States without admission or parole. The respondent requested no relief from removal. The respondent, through present counsel, requested an order of removal with the intention of requesting deferred action from the Department of Homeland Security. Consequently, the Immigration Judge entered an order of removal.
 
On appeal, the respondent’s present counsel argues that the respondent’s previous accredited representative made the admissions of allegations and concession of removability without the respondent’s authority. He also claims that the accredited representative misled counsel concerning whether pleadings had been taken. He alleges that this “foreclosed the respondent’s established legal strategy.”
 
This Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1 (d)(3)(i); Matter of R-S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 629 (BIA 2003); Matter of S-H-, 23 I&N Dec. 462 (BIA 2002). This Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment, and all other issues raised in an Immigration Judge’s decision de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii); Matter of A-S-B-, 24 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 2008).
 
On review of the record, we find that the respondent was represented by an accredited representative after venue was changed to New York. Pleadings had not been taken prior to the change of venue. At the first hearing in New York, the accredited representative requested to have pleadings put over to prepare a suppression motion. The Immigration Judge granted the requested continuance. See Tr. at 18-23. At the next hearing, the accredited representative said he intended to request a deferred action in light of the fact that the respondent is suffering from end stage renal failure. At that hearing, the accredited representative admitted the factual allegations and conceded removability. The respondent was present at that hearing and at no time expressed any objection to either the planned request for deferred action or the admissions and concession made on his behalf. See Tr. at 25-27.
 
At the next hearing, substitute counsel Diaz appeared. He told the Immigration Judge that the respondent intended to take an order of removal and then request a deferred action. He said that no voluntary departure was requested but did request additional time before the entry of the order to enable him to prepare the packet of medical records needed for the deferred action request. That request was granted. See Tr. at 29. At the final hearing, counsel presented a suppression motion that was denied. See Tr. at 34. Counsel then requested the removal order be entered. See Tr. at 35.
 
*2 Present counsel is in effect alleging that the respondent received ineffective assistance of counsel from the prior accredited representative. However, the prerequisites for allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel have not been fulfilled. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 472 (BIA 1996); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Moreover, the respondent has failed to identify how the admissions and concession negatively affected the outcome of the respondent’s case. The claimant must establish that he suffered prejudice as a result of his accredited representative’s ineffectiveness. See Matter of Lozada, supra, at 640. The term “prejudice,” refers to actual prejudice. Matter of Assaad, supra. This means that it is likely that an alien would have prevailed at the hearing or on appeal had the negligent representation not occurred. Id. The degree of negligence that must be established for ineffectiveness is that counsel’s representation was so egregious that it rendered the hearing unfair. See Jiang v. Mukasey, 522 F.3d 266, 270 (2d Cir. 2008); Matter of B-B-, 22 I&N Dec. 309 (BIA 1998).
 
While present counsel says that the admissions of the allegations and concession of removability “foreclosed the respondent’s established legal strategy,” both present counsel and the former accredited representative indicated repeatedly that the legal strategy was to take an order of removal and request a deferred action. At the first hearing where he made an appearance, present counsel did not mention a suppression motion or ask whether pleadings had been taken. See Tr. at 29-31. We find that the prior accredited representative’s decision to admit the allegations or concede removability was a reasonable tactical decision and find no evidence in the record of egregious circumstance requiring the setting aside of the pleadings. See Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377 (BIA 1986). In light of the fact that the respondent had no apparent relief from removal, and in light of the fact that the respondent is suffering from end stage renal failure, it was a reasonable tactical decision to request a removal order with the intention of requesting a deferred action. Consequently, the respondent has failed to show a prima facie claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
Accordingly, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Molly Kendall Clark
FOR THE BOARD
2010 WL 1607055 (BIA)
End of Document

© 2020 Thomson Reuters. No claim to original U.S. Government Works.




2010 WL 1607053 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ANDREA PIMENTEL BRICENO
File: AXXX XX0 695 - Orlando, FL
March 18, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jorge De La Mar, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 This case has been before the Board on two prior occasions; April 27, 2009, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of an Immigration Judge’s denial of her motion to reopen in absentia proceedings, and July 30, 2009, when we denied her motion to reconsider our prior decision. On August 17, 2009, the respondent submitted a motion to reopen proceedings. The motion is denied.
 
With her motion the respondent has submitted a two-sentence statement from her counsel swearing that he “did not receive proper service of the next scheduled hearing date.” It appears from the record that this document was produced in response to an observation in our prior decision that such a statement was not of record. The motion is untimely, as it was filed more than 90-days after the Board’s April 2009 decision. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The respondent also has not demonstrated that this statement was previously unavailable, and could not have been presented in conjunction with her original motion to reopen. We observe that the respondent has neither alleged that she had ineffective assistance of counsel in this regard, nor complied with any of the requirements for such a claim. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).
 
Given this record, we decline to exercise our discretionary authority to sua sponte reopen and remand proceedings for consideration of counsel’s statement. Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(a), (c). We do not find that the piecemeal presentation of evidence in response to noted deficiencies in proof supports reopening of the proceedings. In addition, this document fails to overcome the fact, as noted in our prior decision, that the Immigration Judge’s decision reopening proceedings and the notice of hearing were not returned as undeliverable by the Postal Service. Therefore, we find that reopening is not warranted. Id.
 
Accordingly, the motion is denied.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD

2010 WL 1284455 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: TABUGBO THEODORE ADOGU
File: AXXX XX5 272 - Baltimore, MD
March 16, 2010
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Karla S. Baker
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on March 22, 2005, when we affirmed the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen proceedings following an in absentia order of removal dated April 14, 2004. On July 28, 2009, the respondent flied a motion to reopen proceedings. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has filed an opposition to the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
With certain exceptions, an alien is entitled to file only one motion to reopen and the motion must be filed not later than 90 days after the final administrative order of removal. See section 240(c)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The respondent’s current motion is a second motion to reopen and it was flied with the Board more than 5 years after the final administrative order of removal was entered.
 
The record establishes that neither the respondent, nor his counsel appeared at the respondent’s April 14, 2004, hearing. The respondent was ordered removed in absentia. A motion to reopen and a Notice of Appearance (Form EOIR-28) was filed with the Immigration Judge on June 16, 2004, by Attorney Patrick Tachie-Menson. The motion claimed that the respondent was ill on the day of the hearing, that he had terminated services with his prior attorney and had not yet retained Attorney Tachie-Menson, and that he did not know how to notify the Immigration Court about his illness. Attached to the motion was letter from a doctor dated May 18, 2004. The Immigration Judge denied the motion to reopen, and this Board affirmed that decision on March 22, 2005.
 
The respondent’s current motion to reopen was filed more than 4 years later. The respondent now claims that he had retained the services of an attorney, Lloyd Ukwu¿FN1º prior to his hearing, that Mr. Ukwu knew about the respondent’s back problems and said he would appear in Immigration Court on behalf of the respondent, that the attorney did not appear, and that the respondent only discovered this fact after he had been ordered removed in absentia. The respondent states that he received a document from the Immigration Court, took it to Mr. Ukwu for explanation, and was told he had been ordered deported and would have to file a motion to reopen. The respondent states that he was later visited by Mr. Ukwu and Mr. Tachie-Menson, and that he was told to write a letter, based on what was dictated to him, and to sign it. The respondent reports that on June 11, 2004, Mr. Ukwu contacted him again about an urgent need to come to his office, pick up a letter, and have it notarized. The respondent states that he did what he was told, even though he knew it was not in his best interest.
 
*2 The respondent reports that after the June 11, 2004, incident he never again heard from Mr. Ukwu. The respondent states that he called the attorney’s office and also visited there, but was told Mr. Ukwu was out of the country. Later, the respondent’s work permit expired and he sought advice from other attorneys. Based on this advise, the respondent filed a complaint against Mr. Ukwu with the District of Columbia Bar on February 22, 2007. On April 30, 2009, the respondent filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge, but it was rejected due to lack of jurisdiction. On July 28, 2009, the respondent filed the motion that is before us now. In his motion he claims that Mr. Ukwu provided him with ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
The respondent’s motion was filed many years after the final administrative order of removal and is untimely. Further, the respondent does not benefit from equitable tolling because he has not demonstrated, or even alleged, that he exercised due diligence in seeking reopening. See Chao v. Virginia Department of Transportation, 291 F.3d 276, 283 (4th Cir. 2002) (equitable tolling is not available when a party fails to exercise due diligence on his own behalf). The respondent was ordered removed on April 14, 2004. He last heard from Mr. Ukwu, the person he claims provided the ineffective assistance, in June 2004. He states that he knew that the documents filed with the Immigration Judge in June 2004, were not in his interest. He did not attempt to seek reopening until 2009. Notably, the respondent filed his complaint with the District of Columbia Bar at the beginning of 2007, more than 2 years before his motion to reopen was filed with the Board. The respondent provides no explanation for the significant delay. Thus, due diligence has not been established in this case, and equitable tolling does not apply.
 
Additionally, we agree with the DHS that the respondent has failed to submit a statement addressing the scope of his agreement with Mr. Ukwu, a requirement for ineffective assistance claims. See Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639. He has also not made any ineffective assistance allegations against the attorney who was actually his attorney of record in the Immigration Court, Mr. Tachie-Menson. The respondent has not supported his motion with a signed or sworn statement, as required by Lozada, and he has also admitted to signing a statement, attached to his original motion, that contained false allegations.
 
For these reasons, the respondent’s untimely filed motion to reopen will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

On March 2, 2010, this Board issued a final order suspending Mr. Ukwu for 5 years based on his disbarment in the District of Columbia.
